ADVERTISEMENT. 



THE present volume, as will be at once apparent to the reader who 
glances at the numbers of receipts, constitutes a part of an AMERICAN 
Hotel Cook Book that is being published serially; the preceding parts 
bound together form the volume called the AMERICAN PASTRY CoOK that 
was published a year ago, at two dollars. That volume has met with a very 
encouraging degree of success, so that a second edition has become necessary, 
and the sales have steadily increased as the merits of the work became better 
' known. 

In the meantime, the most numerous and frequent inquiries have been 
received for instructions how to cook oysters and how to cut meats, questions 
which this book answers in a most thorough manner. Instructions for meat 
cutting, as practiced by hotel cooks, have never before been put in print, it is 
no wonder, therefore, that the demand for them has been urgent; and almost 
the same thing might be said of oyster cooking and all the other matters so 
far as regards hotel and restaurant practice, for the present is the first endeavor 
ever put forth to describe the conditions that really exist in hotels and make 
the cooking directions conform to them and not to a set of strange condition?* 
that exist somewhere a long way off. 

The merit most particularly claimed for the entire series is the reliable 
character of the directions, which offer to any intelligent person a chance to 
learn a trade that is an exceedingly good one for those who have the capacity 
to become skillful in it. 



SECOND EDITION. 



Since the above was first printed another part has been supplied, the 
Hotel Book of Soups, Entrees and Bills of Fare, which adds con- 
siderably to the value of this volume. It is the intention, when the series is 
entirely complete, to bind the whole in one large book, to be called the 

American Cook.

NOTES TO THE FOURTH EDITION. 



The pleasing necessity having arisen of print- 
ing a new. edition of Hotel MtU CooHngy which 
will bring it up nearly even with the American 
Pastry Cook^ although it is a year or two 
younger, I feel bound to return a word of 
thanks for the liberal patronage and support 
thus far accorded me by the hotel fraternity in 
this endeavor to produce a set of books partic- 
ularly suited to their wants. It might not be 
worth while to make any fuss about a new 
issue of any book intended for the unconcerned 
public in general, but amongst us, as a class, 
Ihere is a sort of fellowship which makes itself 
felt when there is occasion for it, and in pen- 
ning these lines I feel that I am writing to a 
great number whom I have reason to regard as 
personal friends, and these will surely be inter- 
ested in a few explanations now to be given. 
Sometimes I have had it in mind to write the 
story of a book — of these two books — but as 
that may never be carried out, it may be as well 
stated here that when the Pastry Cook (which is 
the first volume) was first begun, it was gener- 
ally regarded as the height of folly, the most 
visionary notion possible, to think that hotel 
workers — hotel cooks — would ever buy books 
enough to pay for the printing. I believed 
differently, knowing my fellow workers better, 
and now cannot resist the opportunity of calling 
attention to the results; It is true, of course, 
that the first volume was refused by the pub- 
lishers, and on the grounds stated above; how- 
ever, they were right and it was fortunate that 
it was refused, for it was then a dry and tedious 
book, which went into the most minute details 
with too much care ; it had to be written over 
again and was greatly changed before it was 
finally issued in book form. These two vol- 
umes are in reality one t>ook; the pastry part is 
first, instead of last, as all other cook books 
have it, because circumstances formed it that 
way. There was most need for a hotel pastry 
book, for there was no such book then in ex- 
Utence — plenty of confectionery, baking and 
domestic books, but nothing specially for hotel 
pastry cooks, and there was no opposition to be 
encountered in that line. It required more 
daring and combativeness to issue this, the first 
work on American meat cooking, intended for 
men coo)^s, and, not being of a contentious dis- 
position, I deferred it till the last. The " salads 



and cold dishes" are in the first volume for 
much the same cause. Hotel methods and 
hotel bills of fare are considerably changed 
from what they were ten years ago, and it it 
gratifying to observe that there is much less 
sham and pretense about our business than 
there used to be, less of dishing a dozen foreign 
entrees with great names all out of the same 
pot and a dozen different sorts of game all 
carved off the same leg of venison, and there it 
more plain language and sincerity and better 
cooking. 

Brown Gravy and Espagnole.— One of the 
hardest things to learn without practical ex- 
ample seems to be the making of good pan 
gravy. Every cook, and every one who wants 
to be a cook, should practice making both 
espagnole sauce, as directed in Creole Cookery in 
the latter part of this book, and also the com- 
mon favorite brown pan gravy in the roast 
meat pans. Some old cooks use espagnole to 
a ridiculous extreme ; they get so in the habit 
that they will scarcely let anything g^ into the 
dining room without covering it with this 
sauce, and, having a saucepanful of it ready, 
they make almost as many different dishes out 
of it as cooks used to dish entrees out of one 
pot It is useful in its place, but the greater 
number of strange people who come to the 
hotels dislike the taste. Make it in small 
quantities; use it reasonably, instead of using 
so much butter for gravy and seasoning, but 
not for everything. The grease in the roast 
meat pans Is not gravy. I{ you skim that off 
and thicken the gravy that is under it that will 
be of some use, but it is not perfect; the right 
way is to bake the gravy pan until the watery 
part is all dried out, then the fat is clear as oil 
and can be poured off to the last drop, and the 
light brown coating that sticks to the pan is th» 
real gravy that has dripped out of the meal 
while roasting. Let this be a medium browi| 
color, then pour a dipperful of water to it} 
thicken it slightly with flour or starch, and nai 
it through a fine strainer. This, if of a good 
color and not too salt, is the real principal part 
of espagnole, but without Its high flavor. II 
you add some strained tomatoes to it, you have 
a sauce nearly the same as espagnole and 
that is more useful in cooking for the general 
public. 



THB AMEBICAN OCX)K. 



Some Common Errors in Cooking Meat.— 

It is one of the leading principles of American 
as also of English cookery that cooked meat, 
especially roasted meat, is at its best only when 
it is just done, and is spoiled If allowed to cool 
and afterwards warmed up again* But a great 
many old-style cooks make a regular practice of 
cooking their meats as early as they possibly 
can, and then set them around anywhere ; they 
roast chickens and turkeys, and actually let 
them get cold, so that they may be easier to 
carve ; they cut them up at leisure an hour or 
two before dinner, and set the panful of pieces 
away in any convenient corner, and if we say a 
word in remonstrance they reply: "Oh, that 
will be all right! I put the piece of meat on the 
dish and pour hot gravy over it, and it*s warm 
enough.'' Cooks who do that way evidently 
have no epicurean taste of their own ; they are 
not of the sort called born cooks ; they do not 
know what the meat loses in flavor by being 
treated that way, and there is not much use in 
trying to explain to them. They cannot make 
any sauce or gravy that can equal the fine flavor 
of the outside of roasted meat when it first 
leaves the fire. When Brillat Savarin wrote 
** Cooks are bom, not made, but it requires 
genius to roast,'' it is very likely that he had just 
been struck with a piping hot roast promptly 
sent in from the ratisseriey different from his 
usual run of sauced and lukewarm meats, and he 
thought that was the work of ** genius" which 
was only a lucky accident. Instead of the 
meats being roasted first, the range being re- 
quired afterwards for pastry or other things, let 
these come in first; have the meats ready, but do 
not put them in to cook until it is late enough 
for them only to get done by dinner time. 

Seasoning Beeflsteaks.— Have fine salt and 
pepper mi}t^ed together and dredge a little on 
each steak and chop after it is broiled, not be- 
fore. It is commonly believed that to salt 
meat before broiling makes it dry and hard. 
The way generally in vogue In the high-pricec 
hotels, instead of the above plain seasoning, is 
to have a bowl of fresh butter with some finely 
minced parsley and some lemon juice stirred 
into it, soft but not melted, and spread a little 
of that on each hot steak or chop as it leaves 
the grill. That is cold mailre (Thoiel sauce, 
and all those steaks therefore a la maitre 
d'hOtel. 

Alx>at Eggs. — "Country scrambled" eggs 
are but stirred about in the frying pan while 
cooking; the French cook's scrambled eggs are 
beaten up with a little milk, then stirred about 
in the pan till cooked, like a soft omelet with- 
out" any brown color. There are conflicting 
ideas about omelets, many cooks in the best 



hotels making them without adding milk be* 
cause they are tougher and easier to make up 
quickly that way ; but the addition of a spoon- 
ful of milk makes the omelet short and tender, 
and, consequently, better eating. Some think 
that salt added to the eggs before cooking 
makes the omelet heavy and they use none ; a 
more likely cause of heaviness is too much 
beating of the eggs; it makes them rise in the 
pan, but they fall again of their own weight 
before they reach the table. It is best to beat 
the eggs only just enough to mix them thor- 
oughly. A most useful hint for cooking cg^s 
in haste for a crowd, such as a party of excur- 
sionists or summer resorters, who ail come in 
at once, will be found in " steamed scrambled 
eggs" on page 304. 

Practice Salad-making. — Young cooks who 
commence and learn in small hotels — ^as the 
majority must — have but little chance to learn 
to make good salads from the simple fact that 
salads* are not among the common popular 
dishes, and the average public seldom seems 
to think of them. Then, when the cook is 
promoted, after a while, to work in a hotel or 
restaurant patronized by a more appreciative 
and cultured class of people he is at a loss to 
meet their requirements because they expect 
salads and sauces and various dainty trifles both 
hot and cold which his former employers, per- 
naps, never set the slightest value upon. A 
cook need not enter upon the unthankful task 
of undertaking to teach people what to eat, but, 
for his own sake, to fit himself to take higher 
positions, he should introduce a little salad very 
frequently; if not allowed a separate place in 
the bill of fare, it may even be included under 
the head of entrees, though more properly in 
the cold dishes. The first step towards making 
good salads is to become thoroughly proficient 
in making mayonnaise salad dressing. 

This is not at all diflicult, and is only trouble- 
some because there are so many things to be 
got together, but if these ingredients can be 
kept always ready in one place, the time of 
making can be very much shortened, for it is 
the going to one side for qil, here for eggs, 
there for vinegar, yonder for lemons, salt, 
mustard, bowls, whisks, cayenne, sugar, and, 
perhaps, ice, that makes the simple operation 
waste away half or three quarters of an hour 
of valuable time before the salad itself is even 
begun. 

We will suppose that the head cook of every 
first-class hotel can make mayonnaisf* easily 
enough, if he cares to, but, as matter of fact 
this job is generally turned over to some one or 
other of the assistants, and they often have 
great trouble in their attempts to get the 



THE AMBBIOAN OOOK. 



la 



mayonnaise stlfE enough, to say nothing of the 
failures which are complete, and which result 
in their throwing a quart or two of expensive 
stuff away, and having to " try again.** In one 
hotel, where I was employed as pastry cook, 
and had nothing to do with the salads, I could 
not help feeling sorry for the boys who had to 
make two quarts of mayonnaise every fore- 
noon for the I o^clock lunch, and for their deep 
anxiety, as to whether.it "would come,** and 
their gf eat joy and relief when it " did come,'* 
and at the risk of deeply offending the head 
meat cook I told the two boys one morning 
that if they would start their mayonnaise with- 
out putting in any salt at the first beginning I 
would show them a trick. Accordingly when 
they had stirred about one-third of the oil into 
the raw yolks, no vinegar nor anything else 
having 1>een put in, I stepped up and threw in 
the requisite tablespoonful of salt, and the effect 
was the mayonnaise thickened up at once so 
firm they could scarcely stir it. Then I told 
them to begin thinning it with vinegar by a 
teaspoonful at a time; then add oil, then the 
juice of a lemon, then vinegar, then oil, and so 
keep on until they had enough mayonnaise for 
the day ; to remember that oil makes it thick 
and vinegar makes it thin, and that it should 
be kept thick enough to be spread on top of a 
salad with a knife ; and to observe that mayon- 
naise becomes thicker with standing on ice a 
few hours, therefore should be made early in 
the day. They never had any trouble making 
this dressing after that. A teaspoonful of dry 
mustard in the bowl along with the six or eight 
yolks of eggs is an aid in stiffening the sauce, 
and is also a good seasoning for it, but to soften 
the mustard flavor a tablespoonful of sugar is 
required to be added at the finish. At starting, 
the oil should be added only a tablespoonful at 
a time. The best implement to stir with is a 
bunch-of-wire egg beater, but it is a mistake to 
whip up the mixture, it should only be stirred 
around. 

To Make Lobeter Salad with mayonnaise, 
mince celery and lobster in about equal quan- 
tities, but mince separately, not make hash of 
them, as I have seen it done in a chopping 
machine. They ought, if time can be spared, 
to be cut in very small dice. Then mix them, 
moisten with oil, then with vinegar, pepper and 
salt slightly, put in a good spoonful of mayon- 
naise and mix all. Pile the salad in a dish or 
bowl, smooth over, spread thick mayonnaise 
all over it, smooth it with a pallet knife, decorate 
with cut lemons, or olives, or quarters of hard- 
boiled eggs, or fancy shapes cut out of beets, 
and with sprigs of parsley. Shrimp salad and 
chicken salad are good made in the same way. 



Potato Salad* as ordinarily made, does not 
need mayonnaise. Boil some potatoes for the 
purpose and let them cool. Slice thinly, so 
that the vinegar can penetrate the slices. Mince 
an onion very fine, and throw in, also, as much 
or more minced parsley as there is onion, for 
parsley not only makes the salad look attrac- , 
tive, but it has the property for which it ha» 
been esteemed through hundreds of years o€ 
taking away the smell of onions from the 
breath. Then add salt and white pepper. Pouf 
in oil enough to make the potatoes shine, theo 
vinegar all they will absorb, and mix well by 
pouring from one bowl to another. There 
should not be any surplus liquor in the pan, 
but the salad should be moist as it can be with- 
out such surplus. 

Timbales and Dariole Cases.— For a change 
from the common puff paste patties little case» 
may be made by lining muffin rings or any- 
fancy shape of patty pans with good short 
paste rolled very thin. Fill them with flour to 
prevent rising and losing their shape, and bake 
well done. Then empty the flour out, brush 
out the inside and fill with the patty mixtures^ 
such as oysters or chicken meat in dice, mixed 
in a white or yellow sauce, or partridge, or 
other game, in a brown sauce. Lids can be 
cut out with scalloped cutters to fit these tim-^ 
bale caseSj egged over and baked separately. 

To make the wafer shells mentioned in the 
American Pastry Cook^ put no shortening nor 
raising ingredients in the batter, mix two egga. 
in a quart of milk, a little salt, and nothing else 
but flour. Stir it up with flour to the consist- 
ency of common thickening, a litte thinner 
than batter-cake mixture. Set some lard on 
the fire to get hot, have ready some tin scallojy 
shells, or individual jelly moulds, patty pans, or 
anything that will make a handsome wafer 
shape of individual dish size, dip in the hot 
lard, then dip the outside in the plain batter^ 
drop quickly into the hot lard and it will fry as 
a wafer, thin as cardboard, and when of a fine 
light brown color take it up, pull off from the 
tin shape, clip the edges with a pair of shears 
to remove roughness, and continue frying till 
you have enough. These may be used in alt 
ways that puff paste patties are used and make 
many ornamental changes. 

More Sonps and Entrees, — This volume^ 
Hotel Meat Cooking-^ contains a special line ol 
information for hotel men that is not to be 
found in any other work. I have had the 
satisfaction of hearing men in business sayr 
"Oh, yes, I have that book and it has beeik 
valuable to me in my restaurant (or hotel) busi- 
ness, and has helped me out of many a tight 
place.'* It helps them when their cooks dont

"know much ; it helps them to know about meat 
cutting ; about names and kinds of game and of 
iish, and about unusual orders which traveling

Those who feel the need of more recipes for 
entrees, soups, sauces and fancy styles of pota- 
toes to serve with fish, will find them in my 
later book. Cooking for Profit; the expense of 
obtaining it is only the amount of one day^s 
wages of a good cook, and it is not altogether 
an advertising motive that prompts the above 
remark, for I want all those who have taken

While thanking the generous-hearted hotel 
fraternity and the helpful hotel newspapers, 
the restaurant keepers, confectioners and bak- 
ers, and the great number of very humble 
friends who are spelling their way to wages 
and usefulness, for the patronage which has 
bought out ten editions In all of these booksi 
let me add the following extracts upon the sub- 
ject of book- writing from the ToutVs Companion^ 
because it coincides with my own experience : 

"Nevertheless, we fully agree with Mr. 
Brown that writing, though one of the noblest 
and most fascinating of human pursuits, is a 
very poor trade when compared with the other 
professions.** 
********* 

/•Moreover, the labor required to earn a liv- 



ing is far more continuous and exacting, and 
the chance of steady employment and certain 
wages much less, than in the case of other pro^ 
fessional men. But that is only one side ol 
the question, and to the bom and true author 
the less important side of it 

" If any one writes a book merely for the 
money it may bring, — if the writing of it is not 
one long delight, and its publication an event 
of triumphant joy, — ^it were better far to exer- 
cise some more ordinary vocation. The work 
is, as we have said, intense and continuous. To 
write a really good mag .zine article is a 
month's hard toil, plus ten year's special prep- 
aration. Unhappy is that man who gets noth- 
ing for it but a certain number of dollars a 
page! 

"Our advice to students would rather be: 
Do not write unless the impulse to do so is 
irresistible. Writing is a bad business; it is a 

high vocation." 

********* 

"The men who fail are those who assume 

the office of herald without having any message 

to deliver. 

********* 
"The reporter who will run his legs off to 
get an "item" in advance of his rivals; the 
editor who does not care if he is voted " heavy " 
if only he can deliver a valuable thought to 
those who will heed it; or the book-writer who 
is conscious that his work will elevate and help 
his fellows, — ^has a far higher reward than sil- 
ver or gold. His work is its own reward."

803. To Dress TerrapizL 



When there is a qnestion of quantity required for 
a given number of persons it may be counted about 
the same as of young chickens. As th^y ordinarily 
run a terrapin weighs f^om two to four pounds 
alive. There are larger and better, but rarely ob- 
tainable away from the source of supply. The 
amount ef meat iii a terrapin is not over half the 
live weight. It is most serviceable stewed or in 
soup. 

Drop the terrapin alive into a pot of boiling 
water. At the end of 15 minutes take it out and 
take off the bottom shell by chipping through the 
thinnest part where it joins the back shell between 
the openings. This can be done with the heavy 
handle end of a stout knife. Cut close to the shell, 
not to bring any meat away with it. Pour away 
the water that will be found inside but save the 
blood that collects in the deep shell afterwards. 
The gall about the size of a cherry, will be seen near 
the center and must be taken out without breaking; 
also take out the single fish-bait entrail. Loosen 
the meat from the back shell and cut through the 
spine bone that attaches to the shell at a point above 
the tail. Empty it into a pan. When all are done 
go over them, take off the beads and put them with 
the shells for soup; separate the hind and fore feet 
(or £ns, as some call them] making four pieces; 
trim off the daws and scrape off the thin outside cuti- 
cle. It is worth while to take off the the rich fat 
that will be found at the shoulder joints of the 
female8,because it boils away while meat is cooking 
tender, and should be added later. It is of a very 
dark green oolor, «lmost black. Preserve along 



with it all the eggs both large and small. Keep all 
the pieces of meat, fat and eggs in cold water. Put 
on the heads, shells and remaining scraps in water 
enough to more than cover, boil slowly for two or 
three hours, skimming when it first boils, then 
strain the liquor or stock into a clean saucepan,pui 
the pieces of terrapin in and boil tLem one hour. The 
pieces that were like india-rubber at firist will begin 
to be tender by that time, but before being finished 
as stew or soup or otherwise should be taken up on 
dish to OGol and the liquor strained into a bowl. 



Most of the terrapin soup now made in hotels is 
of canned terrapin. The raw article prepared as 
above directed is now the same as the canned, ex- 
cept that the latter has the meat and jelly mixed 
together. Both can be finished for table in vari- 
ous ways. 



804. Terrapin in Shell, Maryland Style. 

Take a baking pan large enough to hold as many 
terrapins as are wanted, halt fill it with dry gravel 
or sand and make it hot in the oven. Kill or stun 
the terrapins, wash offand bed them back downwards 
in the pan of gravel. Bake nearly an hour. Take 
hold with a towel and pry off the belly shell, remove 
the gall bag and the single fish bait entrail Arom the 
inside and loosen the meat from the shell without 
taking it out. Work a cupful of soft bntter and 
half as much flour together with a large teaSpoonful 
of black pepper, the same of salt and juice of lemon. 
Drop a spoonful into each terrapin and replace them 
in the oven for the seasonings to cook. Serve in 
the shell on a folded napkin.

806. Stewed Terrapin. 

The meat of six terrapint—aboat four quarts. 

The liquor or stock — about four quarts. 

6 ounces of butter. 

8 tablespoonfuls of flour. 

Herb and spice seasonings. 

A cupful of sherry or maderia. 

The terrapin and the liquor it was boiled in being 
ready add a little water to the latter to allow for 
boiling away and set it on to boil with a tablespoon- 
ful of bruised peppercorns, a piece of parsley root, 
a small bunch of parsley and green thyme, eight 
cloTes.a b'ade of mace and a tablespoonful of onion. 
Stir the butter and flour together oTcr the fire until 
it is yellow or light brown, add it to the boiling 
stock and also a tablespoonful of salt. When boiled 
sufficiently with the thickening in it strain it into 
a clean saucepan. Thai is the sauce. Tase the 
pieces of cooked terrapin and chop off all the pro- 
jecting points of bones and otherwise trim the 
joints smooth and shapely, then put them into the 
sauce to simmer at the side of the range. Add the 
wine, and the fat pieces if any saved, also the e|ys, 
and strew them over the surface of the stew when 

served. 

806. Soft Shell Grabs, Boiled. 

Every part of a soft shell crab is eatab'e, shell, 
claws and all, except the sand pouch on the under 
side, but the small claws should be taken off when 
the crabs are to be cooked by boiling. 

Drop the crabs into boiling water already well 
salted, cook 10 or 16 minutes, drain,and serve with 
a sauce at the side. 

Tomato ketchup, mayonaise sauce, hot cream 
sauce or butter or parsley sauce are suitable kinds. 

807. Soft Shell Grabs, Fried. 

Bread it in the usual manner by dipping in egg 
in which a small proportion of water has been 
beaten, then in cracker meal. Drop two or three 
at a time into a saucepan of hot oil or lard and fry 
light brown in about ten minutes. The claws should 
be crisp enough to break. Garnish with fried pars- 
ley and serve mayonaise at the side separately. 

808. Osrster Stew for Fifty. 

A quart of small oysters bulk, <*solid meat," con- 
tains eight dozen. 

A quart of selects bulk, "solid meat," contains 
four to four and a half dosen. 

A can of selects contains three and a half dozen 
generally. 

The ordinary conventional oyster stew such as 
people expect to receive when it is cooked to order 
IS a pint bowl nearly full, consisting of one dosen 
oysters and one or one and a half cups of milk or 
milk and oyster liquor. To serve this according to 
the letter a pint of small oysters should be sufficient 



for four stews, or a can of selects for three and a 
half or four; but as the stews for a large number 
are dipped up and guessed there is always a discrep- 
ancy, and a pint can only be relied upon for three 
dishes. Proceeding upon these calculations, for a 
dinner, or oyster supper,or church festival or other 
such occasion provide for 50 stews 

9 quarts of small oysters, or 

12 or 13 quarts of selects, or 

14 or 15 cans of selects, and 

1} gallons of milk. 

i gallon of oyster liquor or water, 

1 pound of fresh butter, 

2 tablespoonfuls of salt. 

Set the milk on in good time that it may heat 
gradually and not burn, and put in the required 
amount of salt which also helps to prevent burning. 
Cook the oysters separately and tfdd them to the 
milk afterwards. It is better to boil the o^bster 
liquor or water by itself and then drop the oysters 
in, because if they are set over the fire cold in bulk 
the bottom will cook hard before the top is warm. 

As raw oysters do not drain well some boiling 
water should be poured over them, the liquor will 
then run abundantly and can be boiled and skim- 
med, the oysters added and then the milk and 
butter. 



809. To Fry Oysters Without Effffs. 

Mix cracker meal and flour together about a cup- 
ful of each, but the cracker meal the larger measure. 
If no cracker meal to be had crush some oyster 
crackers and put them through a seive. 

Have some milk in a shallow pan. Dip the 
oysters out of their own liquor into the mixed meal 
and flour, out of that into the milk, then into the 
meal again, giving them a double breading. It does 
no harm to let them lie in the breading a while. 
Handle carefully not to rub off the coating in places 
for the grease to get in. 

Have enough lard very hot in a deep pan to quite 
cover the oysters. Drop in a few at a time, f^ 
three or four minutes, take out with a skimmer 
when light brown and drain free from grease be- 
fore sending them in. 

The milk causes them to fry to the same fine 
brown color as if eggs were used. If they are quite 
immersed and fried quickly in lard that is hissing 
hot they come out crisp and dry, yet ftill of the 
juice of the oyster. If simmered in lard that is 
only half deep enough the breading peels off like so 
much greasy pudding. 

810. Glam Fritters Without Effffs. 

The same as the oysters preceding, using raw 
clams, but bread them four times over instead of 
twice and dip them in clam liquor until the last 
time when dip in milk for color. They are better 
done this way than with egg breading or batter.

811. A Half-SheU Roa.st. 

» 

Choose oysters in the shell of a good shape for the 
purpose. Open them and preserve the liquor with 
them in the deep shell. Squeeze a little lemon juice 
into them, add salt, cayenne and a small pieoe of 
butter and set them on the hot top of the range 
vntil they begin to boil over. Or, for a little better 
way, set them in a pi^n haying salt in to hold the 
shells level and bake them five minutes in a hot 
oven. This is a good way for clams. 



812. Truffled Oysters. 

4 dozen of the largest oysters. 
1 can of trufSes (fl.50 size.) 

6 ounces of breast of chicken, oooked. 
8 ounces of fat salt pork, raw. 
Red or pickled pepper. 

5 eggs. Flour. Toast. 

Mince and then pound to a paste the chicken and 
salt pork and add a quarter pod of red pepper 
minced very small and a pinch of salt. Gut the 
truffles to the size of peas and mix them in. Lay 
the oysters out on a napkin, insert a penknife at 
the edge and split each oyster up and down inside 
without making the opening very large, then push 
in a teaspoonful of the truffle forcemeat. 

As the oysters are stuffed lay them in flour and 
ooat well with it, then dip in beaten egg in a plate. 
Drop a few at a timb into hot oil or lard and fry 
for three or four minutes. The lard should be deep 
enough to immerse them and hot enough to hiss 
sharply but not smoking. When the oysters are of 
a golden brown take them up and drain on blank 
paper in a hot place. Dust with fine salt. 

Cut diamond shaped pieces of thin dry toast and 
serve four oysters laid diagonally on each slice. 



813. Stuffed Oysters, Broiled. 



Grate the yolks of hard-boiled eggs — four or five 
for every dozen of the largest oysters — mince half 
as much fat salt pork or bacon and mix in, also 
black pepper and chopped parsley. Add a raw 
yolk to make a paste of it. 

Split the oysters inside by moving a penknife up 
and down without making a very large opening at 
the edge and stuff them. Dip them in fine bread 
crumbs that have been minced and silted through a 
eolander, then into butter melted on a plate, then 
into the bread crumbs again and broil them over a 
elearfire. (See No. 831.) 



As a matter of economy it is well to remember 
that where herd-boiled eggs are needed it answers 
about as well to break the eggs raw,save the whites 
for ether purposes and poaoh the yolks alone in a 
ahallow pan of water. 



814. Oysters with Maoaroni— Mileuiaise. 

1 pound of macaroni. 

3 cupfuls of oysters, or two cans. 

i pound of butter — } cupful. 

1 pint of milk. 

2 eggs. 

1 tablespoonful of flour. 

Pepper and salt. 

It is the same as making macaroni and cheese, 
with oysters in place of cheese. Italians make it 
with fish. 

Boil the the macaroni broken in pieces by itself 
first, throwing it into salted water already boiling 
and allowing 20 or 25 minutes. Strain it in a col- 
ander, put a layer on the bottom of a buttered pan 
and spread half the oysters on it. Drop pieces of 
butter, pepper and salt, more macaroni, the rest of 
the oysters and macaroni on top. 

Mix the flour smooth with milk, add the eggs and 
rest of the milk, pour it over the macaroni and bake 
until it is set in the middle. 

816. Oysters, Box Stew. Bestauremt 

Order. 

In answer to several enquiries as to ''what is a 
box stew?*' it has to be remarked that there Is not 
much sense in the term away from the famous Ful- 
ton market oyster houses. **Box stew" may be found 
on the regular printed bill of fare of a very few 
restaurants, price 60 cents, and when served it is a 
slew of a dozen of the very largest oysters just 
taken out of the shells, with only a spoonful of milk 
and quite as much of the best butter. The oysters, 
are dished upon a small square of buttered toast ia 
a bowl and the rich milk and butter poured around. 
It is uncommonly good eating, although there is no 
reason for the name except that the largest oysters 
that are called eounU in Chicago are called box 
oysters in Fulton market. 

816. Box Pry. 

Read explanation above, and a box fry is a fry^ 
of the very largest oysters in a paper box for 
the buyer to carry home.

819. Oyster Soup. 

1 gallon of oysters — 5 or 6 cans. 

1 gallon of good clear soup stock. 

1 gallon of milk. 

1 pound of best fresh butter. 

1 tablespoonfkil of salt. 

1 heaping teaspoonful of white pepper. 

1 cupftil of crushed oyster crackers. 

The stock is used on the principle that the liquor 
that meat has been boiled in is better than water* 
It should be chicken or yeal broth slightly seasoned 
with celery and parsely and other yegetables, and 
should be taken from the top, clear without sedi- 
ment. 

The things to be guarded against are, not to get the 
milk curdled by boiling it with the oysters, and io 
ayoid haying the scum from the oyster liquor floating 
on top of the soup. To get out of the trouble shift- 
less cooks sometimes throw the liquor away and 
wash off the oysters; of course that makes the soup 
poor. 

An hour before dinner time set the gallon of stock 
on the range in one saucepan and the milk in another. 
Pour the oysters into a large colander set in another 
saucepan on the table and when the soap stock boils 
pour a few ladlefuls into the oysters, stir them and 
let them drain. Then set the oyster liquor thus 
obtained oyer the fire, when it boils skim it,then strain 
it into the soup stock. Next throw in the oysters 
and when they begin to shrink, showing they are 
fliirly hot through take the yessel from the fire. Stir 
in the cupful of rolled crackers, (not crasker meal 
firom the barrel,) the salt, pepper and batter, then at 
last add the boiling milk and pour the soup into the 
tureen. Sprinkle a little chopped parsley oter the 
top. 

It is yery poor polioy to let the oysters oyer reaoh 
boiling heat They begin to shrink and dwindle the 
moment they touch hot liquor and neyer stop till the 
largest saddle-rooks looks like small oysters, and 
the small oysters seem to disappear allegether. At 
least it is hard to find them after the soap has been 
kept hot for some time.

Nothing promotes the enjoyment of a dinner so 
muoh as a happy knack of plain seasoning in the 
cook. Very common food can be made to excite the 
appetite and seem equal to the better description by 
a judicioas use of pepper and salt only. I( is very 
well to talk about leaving it to the people to do their 
own seasoning, but in fhct there is a different taste 
and finer flayor to articles that haye been cooked 
with pepper, especially black pepper, than when 
they are sprinkled with the raw. In combination 
with potatoes, with cooked crackers and with flour 
the difference is especially noticeable. And half the 
people forget or are too indifferent to season the 
things set before them at table; they do not stop to 
analyse the qualities of the yiands but carry away a 
sense that one dinner suits tbem and another does 
not. The question often arises, particularly con- 
cerning oysters. Discretion is needed and to keep 
tn the safe side, but skill in seasoning is a great 
point in cooking. 



824. Oyster Stew. MQk Stew. " 

Cook the oysters and the milk in separate sauce- 
pans. Dip the oysters from the saucepan into the 
bowl, add a ladleful of milk and a small piece of 
Anesh butler. Serye oraokers, butter and shred cab- 
bage separately with the stew. 



Oysters do not always curdle the milk when boiled 
in it, but there is always a danger that they may, so 
the rule is not to run any risk. Besides, to cook 
the oysters in the milk although good for flayor al. 
wa^s makes a diogy looking stew with a scum on 
top. To obtain the best quality and appearance boil 
some oyster liquor separately and keep it ready for 
orders. As it reaches boiling point the scum on top 
can be skimmed cff and aftier that pour it through a 
fine strainer into a clean saucepan, and you haye the 
oyster essence clear and ready for use without detri. 
ment to the appearances. 



It is with cooking an oyster as with cooking an 
egg. It may be either soft boiled or hard boiled, 
only then is the difference that an oyster boiled 
hard is spoiled. To cook oysters for stews set some 
of the liquor that has been boiled on in a little sauce- 
pan and drop in the oysters with a fork. Add a 
pinch of salt and pepper, shake them back and , 



forth while heating and as soon as the liquor ihirlj 
boils they are done. Time about 8 minutes for X 
stew.

829. Fried Oysters. Single Breaded.

minutes. Dish neatly in the middle of a hot platter

863. White Oyster Sauce— Good

fl)ur. Stir up smooth and let it boil It should be

should thin it to the proper degree. As soon as it 
begins to boil again take from the fire Stir in a 
Small lump of cold butler, the juice of the lemon 
and pinch of cayenne, and do not let the oysters 
cook hard.

860. Scallops in Batter ^ Marseillaise. 

Scallops, the shell fish, not being universally 
known are often in an explanatory way designated 
New York scallops in the hotel bill of fare. Scallops

the Atlantic as cockles. Our scallops, not generally 
eaten raw, come to market in bulk without the shells. 
They are the little cream colored lumps seen in 
tubs at the fish shops. They have the tenderness of 
the oyster but the taste of tl.e quabog.

of a large lemon over them, and let remain in the 
pickle an hour or two. Make the fine frying batter

Which of these two mollusks has the best of it 
after all, the oyster, for which the regard is great 
but wholly gustatory, or the clam, for which the re- 
gird is moie than half sentimenta)? Sorry indeed 
we all are that it is so, tor we care nothing for the 
oyster further than that it is good to eat, but a hun- 
dred barrels of oysters may be sold as soon as one 
barrel of clams, and yet one far inland, homesick for 
a taste of the salt sea, will find it in the just opened 
shell of a small quahog or a laige little neck as no- 
where else, and not in the oyster at all, for the latter 
(hriTes most and fattens fastest where there is a mix- 
lure of fresh water with salt But as If to olTset 
this inequality in its market value the clam has got 
itself somehow mixed up with all the New England 
history, tales and traditions to such an extent that it 



can never be left out or disregarded or forgotten, so 
that even if we cannot eat the clam it interests us, 
and we wish it well and grieye because it is not as 
good as the oyster, and neyer can be. To learn 
something more about it let us quote from Thoreau, 
exploring the clam grounds of Cape Cod, like a 
Layard on the site of another Nineveh: 

* 'There were, here and there, heaps of shells in 
the fields where clams had been opened for bait; fur 
Orleans is famous for its shell-fish, especially clams. 
The shores are more fertile than the dry land. The 
inhabitants measure their crops, not only by bushels 
of corns, but by barrels of clams. A thousand bar- 
rels of dam-bait are counted as equal in Talue to 
six or eight thousand bushels of Indian corn, and 
once they were pro3ured witout more Ubor or ex- 
pense, and the supply was thought to be inexhaust- 
tble. *For,' runs the history, *af(er a portion of the 
shore has been dug over, and almost all the clams 
taken up, at the end of two years, it is said they 
are as plenty there as ever. It is even affirmed 
by many persons, that it is as necessary to stir clam 
ground frequently, as it is to hoe a field of potatoes 
because, if this labor is omitted, the clams will be 
crowded too closely together, add will be prevented 
from increasing in site ' But we were told that the 
small olam, mya armaria^ was not so plenty here aa 
formerly. Probably the clam. ground has l>een stirred 
too frequently, after all. Nevertheless one man, whe 
complained that they fed pigs with them and so made 
thom scaree, told me that he dug and opened one 
hundred and tweoty«six dollars worth in one winter, 
in Truro." 

Ag<iin: ^*We found some large clams, of the speciea 
maetra toltditnma, which the storm had torn up f^oai 
the bottom, and east ashore. I selected one of the 
largest, about six inches in length, and carried it 
along, thinking to try an experiment on it. We soon 
after met a wrecker with a grapple and a rope. * * 
He also told us that the dam which I had was the 
sea dam or hen, and was good to eat. We took our 
nooning under a sand-hill, covered with beach-grass, 
in a dreary little hollow, on the bank, while it alter, 
nalely rained and shined. There, having reduced 
some damp drift-wood, which I had picked up on the 
shore, to shavings with my knife, I kindled a fire 
and cooked my dam on the embers for my di«ner; 
for breakfast was commonly the only meal which I 
took in a house on this excursion. When the olam 
was done one valve held the meat and the other the 
liquor. Though it was very tough, I found it sweet 
and savory, and ate the whole with a relish. In- 
deed, with the addition of a cracker or two, it would 
have been a bountiful dinner. I noticed that the 
shells were such as I had seen in the sugar-kit at 
home. Tied to a stick, they formerly made the In- 
dians hoe hereabouts " 

*'The old man said the great dams (long neeksV 
were good to eat, but they always took out a oertaia

be added. 

Trout take from 6 to 15 minutes to fry, according 
to size. Drain them thoroughly from grease in a 
pan tilted up at one end and set in the open oyen. 
Serye a slice of bacon with each fish.

their trout fried in this way. 



866. Boned Trout Fried with Bacon. 



J^Restaurant Order,)

slice of bacon inside, close up and lie the two sides 
together again with two or three turns of twine.

minutes. Drain on a hot pan in the oyen, take off 
the t wine, dredge fine salt over, and «erve in a hot 
di h with fried parsley and quarters of lemon, and 
minced potatoes in a side dish.

880. The Mystery of the Fillet. 

Probably we can do nothing more useful here 
than explain a matter that has never been explaioed 
before in set phrase, and is a source of evident con- 
fusion, not only among some cooks, and others con. 
cerned in cooking, but among the frequenters of 
restaurant and hotel tables. 

The word fillet is used in cookery in its broadest 
meaoing of a band or strip, and is strict in Fence 
ODiy in one point, that it always means meat without 
any bone. It does not always mean the same piece 
of meat in every animil, and it docs not follow that 
because the fillet of beef is the tenderloin that a 
fillet of fish can be called a tenderloin like 
wise. When our big cook books that mystify mare 
than they teach tell us to bone a fillet of veal they 
<io not mean the tenderloin of veal only but the 
whole of the loin, the tenderloin being called the 
under or small fillet. The same with mutton and 
other small meats. A fillet of rabbit is the saddle 
of rabbit with the bone out. A fillet of fowl is 
the breast of fowl, but as that has a natural 
division each fowl furnishes four fillets accord- 
ing to cooks' talk, tbe upper fillet or portion of 
the breast, apd the minion fillet or division cf the 
breast th^t is nearest the breast bone. So with fish: 
the fillet first is the whole side of a fish free from 
bone, but technically any boneless piece of fish of 
considerable size is spoken of as a fillet (strip) of

any style it simply means sides of fish cut off clear 
from the bone. And that is not quite the same as a 
slice or a steak.

8G2. Smelt's Sauteed In Brown Butter. 

Or, Au Beurre Notr, 

Pull out the gills and cleanse the fish, wash and

fish will take over a pound of butter. A very short

only as they are wanted, a panful at a time, adding

them while cooking and they should be sent to table 
before it subsides, with potato pancakes, or oro» 
quettes, or toast in the same dish.

waters. It can be cooked in all the wayi previously 
mentioned. The foregoing does for any kind of

a07. Broiled Salmon Steak with New 

Potatoes. 



One of the greatest luxuries that can be provided 
for a fine breakfast or di ner, not only for its high

fishes. 

Slice the fish with a sharp knife and sever the

it in. 



The above is a la Tuaitre <f hotel by a short order 
method, sui'able for a dish for a pavty, of almost any

mch. It takes at least fifteen pounds {pross of salmon 



for broiled steaks for breakfast for fifty, in hotel

898. Broiled Trout Steak— HoUandaiae

Most, if not all, of the old established favorite 
sauces have c*ime do»n to us in two forms, the orig- 
inal French and the modified English; the pungent 
and the mild; it might almost be said the costly and 
the cheap, but it is too plain that the cheaper form 
is generally liked the better without regard to any 
difference of cost. It may seem trite to say it but it 
is necessary: the cook sh')uld be equally prepared 
and equally willing to make them ei her way, and if 
he finds, as is generally the case, that the cheaper 
form suits beH for the hotel table he can reserve the 
more expensive, or the more concentrated, pungent 
or vinous form for particular or private lequire* 
ments.

butter ; may need thinning down for some purposes^

n delicate flavor but contains more water in its com-

water. It should generally have seasoned stock,

stock sometimes can be used afterwards for fish 
soups or chowders, or for stewing fish steaks in.

923. Potatoes, Hollandaise.

There used to be a Dutch kidney potato of

If the white fish of the lakes were a scarce variety 
it WbUld doubtless be held in the highest esteem. It 
is one of the best of fishes, and from a culinary 
point of view the most desirable in maay ways. 
Firm, bonelcM, a splendid broiler and fryer; deli 
cate'y white when boiled ; takes on the best color in 
the oven, aod is never rejected at the table. And 
yet people d) not habitually boast of it as of the pet 
fishes. A traveler has written this in its praise: 

**l shall never f »rget my first white fish. I had 
set out from New York for Niagara by a night train, 
and having fallen itato sound slumber at Albany in a 
comfortable sleeping car, I did not awake till we stop- 
ped for breakfast at R Chester, near L«ke Ontario,at 
nine o^clock next moroing. Rising hastily fresh as 
a daisy after my good night*s rest, I fouod myself 
shortly in a handsome refreshment room, seated 
before a cup of steaming coffee, a plate of hot cakes, 
and a broiled fish", whioh had been swimmiug un 
suspect ingly in the lake at four o'clock that morniog. 
The feelings of a true epicure who lights upon a new 
delicicy of the fir«t order are indescribable. White- 
fish is a sort of idea'ised mackerel, with a tioge of 
etherial salmon flavor ; rich without gre<isiness, fnll 
without strangness, and delicate without insipidity 
I ate it with unflaggiug appetite every morniog that 
I remained along the whole great chain of lakes and 
rivers, from Chicago to Saguenay, aud every morn- 
ing I thought it rather better than the last If I 
could only succeed in acclimatising it in our owe 
Sottish lochs— where it sti'l lingered within histor 



ical times, and lived freeiy during late geologiciU 
epochs— I should feel (as prefaces always say) that 
my work was not quite in vain."

936. Boiled Slices of Fish. 

We had, a little waj back, some examples of fish 
steaks or slioes broiled with appropriate sauoes; the 
same steaks or slioes oan be boiled as well, and in 
short order, in a frying pan or tin pan, and it is 
Tery useful to remember it as the fish for dinner 
often comes in late. The slices only need to boil 
slowly for from six to ten minutes, and may then be 
served with any of the boiled fish sauoes, with po- 
tatoes in cream sauce, potatoes in butter sauoe, and 
the like. But there should in most oases be season- 
logs in the water the slioes are boiled in. An insipid, 
washed out slice of fish with an imitation sauce may 
satisfy hunger and serve a present purpose but does 
not teod to draw custom. Expensive sauces may be 
out of reach, but plain seasonings for the boiling 
fish itself are always available. A panful of stock 
with an onion, whole pepper, salt, parsley and lit- 
tle vinegir in it, and perhaps some green thyme and 
a scrap of bay leaf, makes a slice of salmon, trout, 
sturgeon, cod, or hslibut, a different sort of dish 
from the same boiled in fresh water — as they often 
are. 

In cold weather the stock left from the previous 
day*B boiling of fish will be good for boiling steaks. 
This mode of cooking needs to be encouraged for 
breakfast as well as for dinner dishes, to lessen the 
frequency of the more expensive mode of f^iog.

939. Baked Gherman Oarp with Green 



Scale and cleanse the fish— always remove the 
head, which otherwise will impart an unpleasant 
taste— and let lie in cold water a while. An hour 
before dinner put them in a baking pan with a little^ 
salt and black pepper and a slice of salt pork for 
seasoning laid on the bottom, and just enough soup 
stock to keep the pan from burning. Bake the fish 
about ten minutes, withdraw it from the oven and 
peel off the skin of the upper side. Dredge a little 
flour evenly all over — stirred through a seive with 
the fingers if you have no dredgei — smooth it with 
the blade of a knife, then bake half an hour and 
baste wi>h butter while it is b%king. The fish will 
be light brown and there will be a small quantity of 
thick and rich sauoe. Having taken up the fish pat 
in some stock, stir up, boil and strain it and mix in 
a pint of green peas, already cooked. Serve the 
sauce and peas on the fish in the plates.

Piquante.

Potato croquettes are made difficult to fry by too 
much milk or water being mixed in when the pota- 
toes are mashed. They then turn soft in the hot 
lard, and become shapeless and greasy. The pota- 
toes for croquettes should be mashed separately and 
kept dry.

064. Baked Blueflsh— Bordelaiae. 

After cleaning and trimming off the fins wash the 
fish and wipe it dry. Fill the inside with the veal 
forcemeat of the next receipt, and sew up with twine. 
Cut up into the baking pan a piece of carrot and

fat from the top of the stock boiler, a ladleftil of 
claret or white wine, some pepper and salt. Put in 
the fish, bake in a hot oven. While it is baking fry 
a slice of ham in butter, add a bastingspoonful of 
flour and let it become brown in the pan for thicken* 
ing When the fish is taken up pour into the pan 
another ladlefol of stock and another of wine, thicken 
with the brown flour, and when it has boiled up in 
the pan strain the sauce into a sauce pan, simmer at 
the side of the range and take off the grease as it 
rises. Add the juice of a lemon and some cayenne. 



066. Wine Sauce for Fish. 

The< above described method of baking a fish in 
wine stock and making a thickened wine sauce for 
it in the same pan, whether the fish be stuffed or 
not, is a favorite one both with the cooks and with 
those they cook for, and is resorted to with nearly 
every kind of fish that comes to the hotel table, the 
designation, a very proper one, usually being <'with 
wine sauce," the cooking wine available usually 
being sherry. Another most popular way is that 
directed for speckled trout with fine herbs (No. 952), 
which is also applicable to every kind of fish and is 
in general practice, it being merely optional whether 
the fish, whatever the kind may be, shall be stuffed or 
not. The skill of the cook, and natural taste ia- 
shown in the appearance of both the fish and the 
sauces, in making the latter of good color, bright, 
and not pasty; things at once appreciated in prac-- 
tice, if not altogether to be made plain in words.

960. Fillets of Fish with Quenelle 

Dressinff. 

We will not specify any particular kind of fish, 
for this is a tasteful and handsome way f jr any

Split the fish in two down the back, take out (he

Then lay the fillets in a bakmg pan, with a little

Serve neat cuts on the fish plates with potatoes^

961. Fish Forcemeat. 

The qnaotity of this receipt is about enough for 
one large whitefish laid open.. It should be doubled 
for fish for fifty. 

12 ounces of whitefish, raw. 

8 ounces of bread panada. 

4 ounces of butter.

Salt, pepper, and a slight grating of nutmeg. 

Any other kind offish will do if whitefish is n(yt 
at hand. See that it is free from bones and skin, cut

and pound them together till smooth. 

This delicate paste is useful in a number of ways^ 
and should be made often enough for the ingredi-

962. Bread Panada. 

Shave (he crust from some s(sle rolls or slices of

963. A Southern Way. 

In the restauran(8 and hotels of the South a oon- 
siderable use is made of coloring pepper for baking 
fish. The receipt for making it will be found in the 
miscellanea at the end of this work. It ean be pur- 
chased in bottles the same as curry powder or file 
gumbo. Though mild in flavor it gives the fish 
the appearance of being covered with a coating of 
cayenne. Split the fish down the back and take 
out the bone. Wash and wipe dry, and lay it in a 
baking pan, skin ride downwards and with butter 
in the pan under it. Dredge over salt end a plenti* 
ful coating of coloring pepper. Pour a ladleful of 
broth in the pan and bake the fish according to kind 
and thickness, from fifteen to thirty minutes.

Id the columns that have preceded this the reader 
now has all the good, straight ways of cooking fish 
for breakfast, dinner and sapper, nearly all the nec- 
essary sauces for both fish and meats, and nearly all 
the ways of cooking potatoes to go with ihem. 

It is to be understood, of course, that the sauces, 
•etc., do not speciaUy belong to the several fishes, but 
are to be changed over to other kinds' according to 
the general intimation of fitness convey ed. 

When a new kind of fish comes along consider 
:first whether you can broil it, either in thin sides or 
«lioes ; the best way for many reasons, but the broil- 
ing arrangements are seldom good enough to accom- 
modate hotel numbers. Then consider the simple 
rolling in flour and frying in real hot lard, and after 
that breading and frying. For dinner give first 
preference to boiling and think over what good-look- 
ing sauce you will make to go with it. It is when 
fish is very plendful and people seem to tire of it 
that the ways like stuffing with oysters and baking 
«ome in, and a little novel <y of method is always to 
t)e kept in reserve for uncommon occasions. 

There are other dishes of fish to be mentioned at 
« future time, of the class of made dishes, which 
we are all gUd to adopt because of the necessity of 
flometimes making a fish entree to fill out the bill of 
fare, especially on Fridays and in Lent ; and because 
there are times when the fish on hand is not enough 
for anything else but a side dish. 



965. Turbot. 

This is a summer fish, in season fh»m early spring 
to late autumn. It runs from four or five to twenty 
pounds in weight, the small ones are considered the 
best. 

Cook a turbot either by cutting the meat, after re- 
moving the bone, in p^ec^s of e^en size and egging, 
breading and frying, or boil it whole in a broad 
kettle and serve a sauce asusual, or bake it in any 
of the ways heretofoce directed for other kinds. 

A turbot purporting to be broiled whole is only 
broiled on the white side, partially, then turned over 
into a baking pan and finished by baking, with a 
buttered paper on top to preserve the appearances 
It must be split down the back to keep the white 
aide from breaking while cooking. 

Tafl's Point Shirley bills of fare proclaim turbol 
king of the sea. Some folks call him only king of 
flat fish— a sort of chief plaice or boss flounder 
or jolly old sole. He is an historical fish, however, 
the great fish story originator, fkmous at the ban- 



quets of the Greex and Bomans, recipient of the beet 
attentions of the most ingenious maitres d^hotel and 
the chiefest of cooks, who delighted in him for his 
accommodating shape for a dish, and broad expanse 
of smooth white surface to draw sunfloi^ers in 
lobs' er coral on. 

It was turbot that caused the death of YateU 
mail re d' hotel and chef-de-cuisine to the great gen- 
eral, the Prince deConde. . The king was coming and 
they were preparing a regular Southern Hotel din- 
ner for him. Vatel sent both to Blackford's and to 
Booth's for a fine turbot to come at the appointed 
time by Wells, Fargo & Co.*s Express, without ask- 
ing the price or caring much what It cost. For Va- 
tel had a special way of fixing up a turbot and 
wanted the king to see it. His way was to cut the 
fish down the back and open it enough to get out ali 
the bones, then fill the inside with fish forcemeat 
(No. 961) and bake it with the white side uppermost, 
with wine, mushrooms and oyster liquor and various 
other things in the pan, basting till the fluids were 
nearly dried up and set in glaze all over the fish. 
When he dished it up he had somfc rich cream sauce 
(No. 982) which he colored light green by mixing in 
some ravigote butter (No. ) and that he poured over 
the fish. Then he took smelts enough to encircle it, 
took out their bones and stuffed them, then formed 
in rings by sticking their tails in their mouths, 
loured and fried ihem and laid them around. Then 
he took some fillets of soles, nice and white, and 
with a fine larding needle worked patterns in them 
with shreds of black truffle and red meat of lobster, 
simmered them in butter and then laid them around, 
and wherever they would do the most good, and 
then sent it in hot. But on this great occasion his 
great specialty could not be produced, he telegraphed 
and telephoned and sent couriers till he was tired. 
The king arrived and no fish had come, and Yatel 
went out and committed suicide. Not in any cold, 
clammy, disgusting way, but respectably as a gentle- 
manly chief cook should, by running his sword 
(hrough himself. 

A high literary lady of France saw the true merits 
of the case, and when most people were laughing at 
Vatel for a fool she wrote pathetically about the af- 
fair and made it look more like a tragedy than a 
joke. The rest of the French cooks were not un- 
grateful but gallantly named a soup after her, and 
consequently we have consomme Seviffne to this day* 
If any one thinks that a comical way of commemor- 
ating a person we ask them to consider how much 
longer the name of the soup will endure than any 
headstone, and how few, comparatively, would ever 
read the name of Madame de Sevigne if it were not 
in the menu. 

Equally scarce, costly and impossible to obtain 
wa9 this fish when a certain Cardinal Fesch unex- 
pectedly received two at once as a present fh>m a 
devoted fHend. Not a hotel or restaurant in the city

968. Sturgeon. 

May be seen in the market of every size, from 
one or two pounds weigL. to two or three hundred. 
The skin studded with ridges of horny substance is 
tough and mubt be removed by means of a knife. 
Cook in steaks same as halibut. T^ke midd'e cuts, 
remove the bone, fill the cavity with stufliog, and 
braise or bake same as meit, allowing plenty of 
time. Also stew and fricassee the same as veal. 
Sturgeon is salted and smoked in large quantities. 
The roe is converted into cavair. In England the 
fish is a rarity and every one caught in the rivers 
belongs to the crown. Sturgeon's head being very 
gelatinous is said to be a good substitute for turtle. 



It is eit remedy probable that the names bestowed 
upon French dishes all had some tradition or histor- 
ical association attached in the first instances. Here 
is a hint from a magazine article of the meaning of 
trout a la Genevese : 

*'At the sime time Charlemagne encouraged the 
production of fruit and flowers, as adding to the 
«njnymeDt of the people, and was pleased when any 
special delicacy was presented to him The Genevese 



trout are honorably mentioned in the capitularies, 
and it will be remembered that one of these trout, 
supplied to Gambaceres by the municipality of Ge- 
neva was charged 300 francs in their accounts." 

969. Trout a la Genevese. 

12 to 16 pounds of trout. 

1 pint of champagne. 

1 pint of sherry. 

1 otiion ftuek with cloves. 

4 ounces of butter. 

A bunch of parsley and thyme. 

A ladleful of broth. 

Salt, pepper, brown roux, toast and lemons. 

Cleanse the trout, take off the heads, wipe dry, 
score through the skin on both sides where the in- 
dividual portions are to be taken off, and also sever 
the bone by striking the point of a knife through. 
Then dredge a little salt and pepper in a baking 
pan, put in the butter, and lay the fish in just touch, 
ing. Add half the champagne and sherry, and if 
there is much vacant space in the pan the ladleful 
of broth likewise, and the onion and herbs. Set in 
a hot oven and baste the fish while it is baking, al-^ 
most onctiintly. The gelatinous grnvy from the 
fish makes a glaze with* the wine, which is to be 
coited over it; by this means till there is no more 
liquid left in the pan. Remove the fish carefully 
into another pan when done, pour the remaining 
half of the wine into the fish pan, boil up, thicken 
with browned flour, and strain the sauce. Serve 
the portions of fish with a slice of lemon on top and 
the sauce poured under. 

A fish cooked in this manner served whole should 
be raised upon a shape of fried bread cut to fit the 
bottom of ihe dish. 



970. Eels. 

Cook either by frying, rolled in floor or breaded,

byside, as so many small fish would be. Vast quan- 
tities of eels are sold in the markets, but only a small 
proportion go to American hotels. The large sorts are 
salted and smoked and find sale in large quantities 
in that form. Eel-pouts, bull-pouts and small cat- 
fish, ready skinned and cleaned likewise, are handled 
by the market men near the lakes in car load lots» 
which shows that the prejudice existing against 
these kinds is not universal. Clear meat of this de- 
scription is, however, generally very cheap — often 
by the barrel only three cents per pound, more gen- 
erally about five cents, and retails at seven or eight 
cents. Whatever good cooking can do to make such 
cheap, solid, and intrinsically valuable food accept, 
able to a larger number than at present unquestion-

071. Salt Mackerel. 

/ As much care, if not skill is needed lo have salt 
mackerel as good as c in be a i for an j thing that is 
done in the kitchen. The particular point is to put 
it on in cold water and remove it from the fire the 
moment it begios to boil. It is then soft and tender, 
but becomes dry if allowed to boil hard. 

To prepare the mackerel for cooking, wash off the 
salt and steep them tweni j-four hours in plenty of 
water, in a yessel set in a cold place. Lay them in 
with the skin side up, that the salt which is heaTier 
than water may escape to the bottom ; and change 
the water once or t wice. 

Scrape out the black skin of the inside of the 
mackerel, cut off the head", divide — if for boiling — 
by cuttiDg in three crosswise, but if for broiling 
split them lengthwise. Hang broiling mackerel up 
lo dry about twenty-four hours before they are to be 
cooked When dished up pour a spoonftil of clear 
melted butter over the pi«ce of fish in the dish. 



It should be remembered by buyers of hotel stores 
that there is t he utmost diffierence in quality between 
the different grades of mackerel, from fat mess to 
the thin and rusiy number foun*, which, if of any 
use at all, certainly are not fit to broil.

084. Sea Bass Baked.

4 pressed cupfuls of fine bread crumbs,

minutes or according to sise. 

Put a little strained tomatoes and brewn gravy

Sardines. 



(Maffazine,) 



**In regard to our food, as in other matters, there 
is no gainsaying the fact that things are not what 
they Be<»m. The dainty little fishes that come U> 
our tables elaborately packed in tiny tin oases, and 
smothered with olive oil, are supposed to have been 
captured where the waters of the Mediterranean lash 
Sardinian shores. In reality they are fiequently 
the product of what are known as the English and 
American Sardine fisheries. Sardines are simply 
the young of the pilchard (clupea pilchardus). They 
get their name from having been formerly caught 
in great numbers off the island of Sardinia. The 
Sardine is, in fact, a member of the herring family, 
and it is only its extreme youth and the manner of 
packing it that separate it from its re'atives.

A Qood Hotel ReM^erator. 



A refrigerator of a convenient medium size 
that is perfectly satisfactory in filling all the re- 
quirements of an ice chest for all purposes* and 
an object of pride to both the proprietor and his 
New Enj^land steward, is in use at the American 
House, Denver, Colo. The diagram below 
shows the form and arrangement. It gives a 
front view as the interior appears When the 
doors are open. The height inside is six feet; 
depth, front to back, five and a half; the middle 
compartment for the ice is three feet wide; the 
cold rooms on each side three and a half. The 
drip from the ice is led away by a zinc drainer, 
and the space below is both dry and cold. The 
outside walls are, of course, double, and filled 
in with eight inches of dry sawdust. This re- 
frigerator is built close by the outer door on one 
side of a cellar basement, the store room being 
directly opposite. It is elevated a step or two 
from the floor.

NOTES TO THE THIRD EDITION. 



"Die immenBc outlay requfred to provide the 
choicest cuts of the best beef for hotel 
three times a day undennlnes many a grand 
ture's buamesB and brings it to ruin. It 
most seriouB strain upon any hotel's revenues, and 
if any method to relieve the strain could be 
pointed out it would of course be adopted with 
avidity and the author of these instructions would 
be only loo liappy to furnish the relief. There 
however, no remedy but to chaise high prices for 
the luxury of choice cuts. These 
written with the belief that "they that are whole 
need not a physician, but they that 
Any person, even a child, can go to the butcher's, 
buy the best he has In the shop, pay Ute hlgiiest 
price, and then sell it at an advance if tliere be a 
bm-er ready. The writer was to diffident to think 
th;tt any advice of hi^was needed in such trans- 
-actions. But In places where these choice cute 
-cannot be purciiased because there are no boarders 
willing to pay enough for them the best methods 
of making use of the cheaper cuts, it was thought, 
might be shown with good effects, and [his book 
is repated as being eminently practical 

It should be observed that our cut of a loin of 
beef shows a long loin, a very long loin, having a 
portion of the solid lean meat of the round at- 
tached. If tt had stlli more of the round it would 
be still more profitable in furnishing a larger pro- 
portion of steaks to the gross weight. One of the 
many stewards of the best high-priced hotels who 
have been pleased to consult these pages has 
objected that no such numt>er of steaks can be 
■cut from a loin and that the statements were 
liable to mislead proprietors and make them dis- 
satisfied with the steward. In proof he mentioned 
that he had purchased a loin of beef that weighed 
70 pounds and only got 40 steaks out of IL His 
was a 4 dollars a day house and that was the sort 
of steaks tliey could serve— alUiough the expense 
evidently olnched even there. But if his short 



loin, which no doubt was choice and fat, co«t 18 
cents per pound the steaks cost 30 cents each and 
it is simply impossible for lower priced houses to 
serve such cuts as he had In mind. Another 
steward, not objecting but simply inquiring, 
pointed to a light and small short loin and said — 
"I can only cut one length off that and can only 
cut it Into such a number of steaks — can you get 
any more? " and the answer was "No more porter- 
house cuts, because they are not there ; but a few 
more out of the flank that are good but are not 
"Brst-class" such as all first-class stewards and 
tirst-class cookr crave." AH that is eiplicitly 
stated in the book, and there can be no miscon- 
ception by anyone who will read. 

Ciianges take place rapidly In these times and 
the past few years have brought about a new 
system of distributing dressed beef in refrigerator 
care to all parts of the country, so that the choice 
cuts of shipped meat may be brought fn some 
pliices cheaper than the native product, and thia 
new system furnishes the relief sought, at some 
seasons of the year making it possible for housea 
of the smaller pretensions to serve as good beef 
as any. Tenderloins, rolls, butts, rack-wasts, and 
short loins of the best quality can often be pur- 
chased resonably In every large railroad town 
where before the butchere had been obliged to 
refuse to cut them out for hotel buyers. 

In the matter of cutting up hams, too, the re> 
marks about city-cut meat apply. The directions 
and illustrations are especially applicable to coun- 
try hams and small packers', scarcely two of 
which are alike, and unpracticed hands make ter- 
rible work of haggling them to pieces; for that 
>n the saw is recommended. But the packers 
make such neat work that the bone In the 
best hams is scarcely as large as the palm of thv 
hand and may be easily removed with the point 
knife without using a saw, and the ham b« 
cut in two with one stroke from end to snd.

HOTEL CARVING. 



Certain men who practise the same set of duties 
every day will acquire extraordinary dexterity in 
their line, and they are the men to watch if one 
Mould learn all the sharp cuts in car%'ing. George 

McG J a Southern hotel keeper was one of 

these fine carvers, but rather off-hand and waste- 
ful with it, for he was full of other business and 
would not dwell upon trifles ; he was for rapidly 
and did not stop to clean all the meat from the 
carcasses; left them to be stripped afterwards for 
salad or hash. There was another man employed 
in his house, a professional carver, Jake Carter by 
name, and one day he said : 

"Mr. McG — how many orders do you think I 
can get off this turkey ?" 

"Oh — I don^t know — how much does it weigh?" 

"Here it is — ^you can judge for yourself." 

"Well — that turkey weighed X2 pounds before 
it was cooked — I suppose I could carve 35 or 40 
orders from it if I was trying, and I don't think 
there are many carvers around here can beat me." 

"Mr. McG — I think I can just about double 
that number for a five dollar bet." 

"FU bet you a ten dollar gold piece against 
seven days wages you can't beat it over ten dishes'* 
— returned McG — and not thinking any more 
about the matter he turned on his heel and went 
off to the front of the house. But Jake Carter 
called witnesses to watch and off that one turkey 
he carved and sent in 70 passable orders. It was 
nearly two years before McG — would pay the 
bet, and then he was greatly in need of Carter's 
services at a banquet and was obliged to pay it ; 
but that was only one specimen of Carter's skill 
as a carver which has been so valuable to him 
that he has had fourteen years constant employ, 
ment in one city, the time being divided between 
only two hotels. 

The art and the difficulty of it consists in slicing 
broad but thin, even as thin as paper. 

Of course there are two sides of the carving 
question and the consumer may not see the sub- 
ject in the same pleasing light that the calculating 
hotel man does ; there must be a medium observed 
that will result in giving satisfaction to the guests 
at table, yet it is so true that nearly all hotel 
dishes contain twice as much as they need and the 
cost of provisions is needlessly increased in that 
way that a skillful carver who can make a dish 
look broad and plentiful without giving it much 
weight may be the most valuable man in the house. 

Some men are not adapted to become good 
carvers ; quick, impatient, irritable men are not. 
It takes an easy-going imperturbable person who 
cuts smoothly with long and steady strokes and 
does not see-saw and scatter the splinters. The 
knives must be thin and as keen as razors ; there 



can be no good carving with the ordinary kitchexfe> 
knives which are used for all purposes, and, withal*, 
the carver must know where the joints are so 
well that his sharpe knives will pass through 
them without contending against the bones. With 
such a keen knife the breast of a small chicken 
can be laid open so as to top cover four dishes^ 
which would not make any showing upon two 
dishes in the hands of a clumsy car\'er, and a 
large chicken will cover six or eight. 

The method is, to place a spoonful of the stuf- 
fing in the dish, slice meat off the drumstic and 
place beside it, or a joint of the wing and on top 
lay the white meat as broad and thin as the size 
of fowl ^^'i\\ allow. 

While such directions may read well enough It 
will be found the practice is not so easy unless 
pursued with steady system. Take up the chicken 
by inserting the fork in the cavity of the neck, 
where the crop was, and keep the fork there in 
one place until the bowl is disjointed and the 
breast is in slices. First take off the legs and 
thighs, cutting to the hip joint, then with a turn 
of the MTist throwing it out of the socket and pass 
the knife clear through, hold up the carcass and 
strike the knife through the small of the back and 
the lower part or side bones fall off. Then re- 
move the wings at the sockets. Next take off 
the first slice — the brown outside — of the breast 
If a large fowl you can get a broad slice more of 
white meat before touching the bone. Next to- 
that is the fowl's shoulder-blade, a bone almost 
like the wishbone, imbedded in the white meat 
and very much in the way of the carver who is 
not posted ; but your knowing hand takes hold of 
zt by the projecting knob, which is the wing 
socket, and gently pulls it off and the white meat 
that comes with it and covers it, and it makes a 
slice itself almost as thin and quite as broad as 
the others. Under that and lying close to the 
breast bone is another larger to be taken off with 
the point of the knife and that makes four slices 
of white meat from one side, eight from the two 
sides, to make the tops of dishes partly made up 
of a slice from the leg, or a side bone or split of 
the upper part of the back. 

In carving a turkey the proceeding is the same 
but less diflScult as there is more meat to work 
on. The usual way, which was the way McG — 
did, is to place the carving fork astride of the 
breast bone and keep it there until the turkey is 
all cut up, but Carter objected that thrusting the 
fork in there cut through a slice of the white 
meat in the best part on each side of the bone 
and he took a hold in the crop cavity as alreadjr 
mentioned.
