THE AMERICAN COOK. 



877 



CREOLE COOKERY. 



BY JESSUP WHITEHEAD. 



Northern men who go south to engage in the 
hotel business place their fortunes in jeopardy if 
they carry with them too much of the misssion- 
ary spirit that assumes that their own section 
alone knows what to eat and how to eat it, and 
that some other section is eager to learn. It may 
chance that the cooks they employ will be the 
means of averting any very heavy disappoint- 
ment, for cooks in general have it thoroughly 
learned that the tastes of the customer are to be 
suited and not their own; but if the cook be 
allowed no discretion, or if he, too, be but a 
stranger to the wants ot the new community, it 
may not be long before the hotel keeper will be 
raying the same old plaint: 

"I TRIED TO GIVE THEIR CITY A GOOD HOTEL, 

a hotel they might be proud of, but the citizens 
would not support me and I lost all." The two 
partners in a hotel in one of the most prosperous 
southern cities said that, when they made an as- 
signment for the benefit of their creditors only a 
few months since. It was true in their case. It 
seemed strange that they should fail while they 
were doing their part so well, but it is possible 
that they differed with the community as to what 
constituted a good table, and the community 
simply let them alone. 

EVERY ITATIOX HAS ITS PREJUDICES 

against some kinds of food ; every set of people 
derides some other set of people for something 
peculiar in their diet, and there is a sensitiveness 
on that subject that is easily touched, so that the 
-setting out of the despised dishes and the omis- 
sion from the bill of fare of the sectional favorites 
may become an actual provocation of hostility be- 
tween the parties concerned. 

The greatest good in life appears to be to a vast 
number of hotel customers in the northern states, 

A JUICY BEEFSTEAK. 

The question of tough or tender seems with 
them to be a greater question than any that arises 
in business or politics or social intercourse. There 
are thousands who will try to drape the world 
around them and the sky overhead in deep mourn- 
ing if they cannot get a juicy tenderloin on de- 
mand. In the hotel business there are men who 
care and men who don*t care, and the men who 
care have the very life worried out of them in the 
perpetual effort to please these beefsteak manies. 
Perhaps one of these has solved the difficulty by 
securing regular shipments of Chicago tenderloins 
packed in ice at a cost of thirty -five cents a pound, 



and freight to be added. He may read, as every- 
body has read, the printed declaration of some 
infatuated beefsteak man that a decent beefsteak 
cannot be obtained in the South, and that a for- 
tune awaits the man who will go and do things up- 
right. But I should hope such a painstaking 
hotel man would not go with his panacea read^ 
packed in ice unless he could be sure of a ready 
run of northern custom, for in any hotel in the 
far South or at any resort sustained chiefly by 
Creole custom or by southern custom, whether 
Creole or not, it will be found that a beefsteak is 
not much thought of; it is but an indifferent ar- 
ticle of diet even when well done and dry; a 
juicy steak, although cooked through, is regarded 
as an abomination , to serve a steak rare done with 
the pink juice of it oozing in the dish, would be 
regarded as a crime against the public health, and 
the consequences to the perpetrator would prob- 
ably be something terrible. I say would be be- 
cause nobody ever does such a dreadful thing 
among the Creoles, and we can only indulge in a 
bare supposition. The Creoles eat beefsteak, of 
course, but as there are plenty of other people 
who drink coffee only for the sake of the cream 
and sugar it carries, and many more who use hot 
rolls only as a conveyance for a large amount of 
costly creamery butter, so the Creoles 

EAT BEEFSTEAKS FOR THE SAKE OF THE SAUCES, 

the vegetables or rice or macaroni or whatever it 
may be that goes with them, and although they 
may not be costly, the mode of preparation of 
these appurtenances becomes the more important 
matter. 

THE CREOLE BEEFSTEAK. 

At one period of my life I was employed as ice 
cream maker to a Creole caterer to Creole custom. 
The neighborhood was entirely French; the man 
and his wife were both Southern French cooks; 
they cooked for themselves and their hands, 
sometimes one sometimes the other, as it hap- 
pened to be convenient. I was supposed to board 
with them. They had their coffee at about six 
in the morning and nothing with it but some 
crackers or bread, all in the usual French style, 
then went to market; had no breakfast untill 
eleven or twelve, and had the principal m«eal of 
the day at six in the evening, and I found that the 
patrons of the establishment all followed a similar 
course. There was a considerable variety about 
the breakfasts; the dishes were always freshly 
co( kftJ, and nearly always were stews made of 
some inexpensive material, but it was in the third 
week before ever a beefsteak was brought into 
the house. It was a good one when it came, 
however, and large if not thick, and seemed 
strangely out of place, lying there with a bunch 



878 



THE AMERICAN COOK. 



c^ magnolia flowers and a pile of mangoes on the 
same bench with okra, egg plant, squashes and 
i.epper8. They used 

PORTABLE CHARCOAL FIRES 

in stone furnaces like buckets, that they could set 
anywhere about. There was a wire broiler hang- 
ing up against the wall. I thought the charcoal 
fire, the broiler and the big beefsteak in the na- 
tural course of events ought to come together, yet 
there was little likelihood that they would. But 
they did. Madame actually crowded that steak 
into that broiler, took it to the fire and broiled it 
brown on both sides. So they were going to sur- 
prise themselves with a real Northern broiled 
beefsteak breakfast, and that at only ten in the 
morning. I looked around for the dish, but 
Madame never brought one; she did not need 
one. When the beefsteak was done she laid it, 
still in the broiler, on top of the hot watertank of 
the cook stove and left it there. While whipping 
up a yolk of egg custard on top of the same stove 
I caught myself foolishly saying to the steak, 
"How did you get here, old aquaintance! and 
aren't you getting pretty dry up there i*" It was 
vret enough after a while, for 

MADAME BROUGHT OUT A BROAD SAUCE- PA^; 

she poured in some oil, she trew in two large 
onions and a head of garlic cut small, and let them 
fry. Then she laid in the broiled beefsteak ; then 
she emptied on top of it three cans of tomatoes, 
added a pod of red pepper and a spoonful of salt 
and let it stew together until twelve o'clock. 
Then she found a dish large enough, fished the 
meat up from the bottom, poured the tomatoes 
over it, set it in the middle of the table, and that 
was the Creole beefsteak. 

CREOLE SAUCES. 

Now, if we take an analytical view of tlie sub- 
ject, we find that the method pursued in preparing 
the Creole beefsteak constitutes the basis, if not of 
all the dishes that differ materially from those 
familiar tp the northern palate. 

"Ha!Ca!" the Creole or Southern French cook 
shouts, making a grimace of disgust when he 
tastes the simple Irish stew or homely pot pie of 
some unsophisticated assistant — *'here, put in 
some onions — plenty of onions, leeks, chives — 
and fry the onions — fry 'em in oil — un'stan'.^ — 
find trow in some garlic — crush it, you can't chop 
Jt, a whole head is not too much — and fry 'em al, 
—then put in your meat, fish, shrimps, crabs, 
chicken and fry — make them taste of something— 
un'stan }" 

And the Creole or Southern French cook must 
h&ye his sauces on hand as regularly as some 
others require a jar of butter. There must be 
first the 



SPANISH SAUCE, THE MODERN ESPAGNOLE. 

The Espagnole of our grandfathers was the- 
same thing that we now call extract of meat; the 
extract of the carcases of a whole ox could be put 
into a gallon jar. The modern Espagnole, or 
Spanish sauce, if left to some listless and thought- 
less assistant to make, as is generally the case, 
comes up little else but a paste made of flour 
browned in grease and diluted with tomato juicev 

SPANISH SAUCE OR ESPAGNOLE. 

But if the skillful cook makes it himself, he wilt 
proceed acct»rding to the method with the Creole 
beefsteak and according to his own directions as 
above repeated; he is going to put the meat or 
soup bones in the pot, and just as Madame broiled 
her beefsteak in order to get the flavor and color 
of the browned meat in her stew, so he will put 
the meat and bones for the Espagnole into a pan 
in the oven and roast them brown before putting 
them in the pot. But if for any reason it is in- 
convenient to roast them so, he will fry them 
more or less brown in the same pot or large 
saucepan with the dozen onions, carrots, turnips, 
pieces of ham and some oil. To be quite explicit,, 
he will fry the onions and other vegetables named^ 
in oil or other fat, anyway, and if he cannot roast 
the soup meat that is to be used in the sauce, he 
will fry it, too, along with them. 

ROUX AND FLOUR THICKENING. 

When that is done, when the contents of the 
saucepan are brown, highly odorous and in danger 
of burning, there arises a point of difference be- 
twixt Creole cooking and the cooler northern 
methods, for the sauce will have to be thick when 
finished, and the oi-dinary way would be to mix 
flour and water to the consistency of cream and 
pour in sufficient to give the required thickness, 
and that would be done at a later stage. But the 
Creole cook abhors water as nature abhors a 
vacuum, which will be easily observable as we 
go on, and instead of flour and water he makes 
roux (pronounced roo) in the same saucepan with 
his fried vegetables ; that is, he puts in as much 
dry flour as the oil or butter or other fat in the 
saucepan will absorb into a kind of paste, and 
stirs that around until it has bubbled and cooked 
and become partially browned, too, before he adds. 

any liquor. 

There has been a paragraph floating around in 
several papers (probably clipped from some well- 
meaning but ill-informed teacher)which confound- 
ed roux with Espagnole. It said roux was obtained 
by roasting marrow bones in the oven, and more 
of the same sort. It is not likely the common 
multitude of cooks will ever learn these funda- 
mental differences, nevertheless some of us must 
keep on saying them over again till we flicker out^ 



7HB AMERICAN COOK. 



ROUX. IS THICKENING. 

If jou mix eQiial quantities of flour and butter 
or oil together and stir it in a pan oyer a fire, as 
soon as it has bubbled up and the flour has the 
cooked taste, you have white roux for thickening 
white sauces and white soups. If you let it re- 
main longer over the fire it becomes brown and 
acquires a different and. if not scorched, a richer 
flavor, and then it is brown roux, and thickens 
brown soups and sauces. So, when the Creole 
cook stirs flour into the contents of his saucepan, 
he makes a brown roux, and immediately after 
pours in a gallon or more of soup stock, which he 
calls bouillon, and adds a quart or perhaps two 
quarts of tomatoes, two bay leaves, a few cloves, 
some salt and whole pepper and allows it to boil 
at the side for an hour or longer and then strains 
it off and sets it away to get cold. 

THE GREAT OBJECTION 

tc the making of roux in the generality of hotels 
throughout this broad land, and which forces the 
cooks to use flour and water instead, is, that to 
make roux uses up a large quantity of butter for 
which there is no visible return, for when the 
Espagnole or Spanish sauce is finished and cold, 
the grease to be taken off is so nearly worthless 
as hardly to be counted anything. Another 
objection is that an inferior grade of butter is 
likely to be furnished for the purpose, and the re- 
sult is that a dozen dishes, sauces and soups, made 
with a proportion of this Espagnole all carry the 
taste of strong butter, just as a dozen dishes, 
sauces and soups of the Creole cooks may all be 
made uneatable for a number of people by an 
excess of tomatoes in his stock sauce. But the 
Creole cook will never use the cheap and harm- 
less fiour and water. He will not take butter if 
he can get oil, and if neither is available he will 
resort to roast meat drippings, and some of his 
imitators driven to the same strait will use a grade 
of grease to make roux that would not bear inspec- 
tion. Espagnole or Spanish stock sauce is not 
peculiar to Creole cookery but is used wherever 
French methods are followed, but the Southern 
French cookery of this country resembles the 
cookery of the south of France and abounds in 
highflavored and pungent dishes, so that the Cre- 
ole cook's Espagnole is almost sure to contain 
twice as much tomato as the same stock sauce in 
the North, and is sure to contain garlic, which 
that in the North has not at all. 

SAUCE CREOLE. 

In any restaurant or hotel in the Creole quarter 
of New Orleans, and at certain restaurants in 
Galveston, if you call for an omelet a la Creole, 
an omelet of the usual appearance will be brought 
in but rolled up wihtin it is a thick mixture of 



tomatoes, onions and garlic, that have been fried 
together. This mixture is Sauce Creole, the cook 
keeps it ready prepared. It is commonly made 
with canned tomatoes, as being the easier way., 
but if specially ordered he will make it this way* 

Peel three or four tomatoes, and cut them up 
in small pieces, cut up a large onion the same 
way, slashing it across and across with the knife, 
while the onion is held in the fingers, and crush 
with the side of the knife three or four cloves of 
garlic. Pour a little oil in a broad saucepan, put 
in onion and garlic and fry light brown, then add 
the cut tomatoes, and stir the mixture over the 
fire until the tomato ju?ce has dried out, and the 
sauce is thick enough to be taken up on a knife^s 
point. Season with salt and pepper while cook- 
ing. 

This is rather strong diet when it is made care- 
fully ; the time that it appears to me to be abso* 
lutely bad, is when the onions in frying are al- 
lowed to burn and make the whole mass black 
and bitter, yet it is in demand for omelets and for 
mixing with many meat dishes in its proper sec- 
tion of countrv, and I have never found that the 
demand was lessened through the presumed bad- 
ness of a burnt sauce. It seems to illustrate the 
necessity of having a variety of dishes for a hotel 
having mixed custom ; such dishes as the Creole 
omelet would be repugnant to the taste of many 
at the hotel tables north, but would be missed and 
wanted by the southern visitor. 

SAUCE TOMATO. 

Pour into a saucepan enough oil to moisten the 
bottom, lay in some pieces of raw ham, and a< 
large onion or two chopped, and fry light brown, 
then put in a half gallon can of tomatoes or raw 
tomatoes equivalent, a head of garlic, few cloves, 
salt and pepper. Motives of economy may dic- 
tate the use of roux to thicken up the full quan- 
tity of tomato juice, but if let alone the Creole 
cook will not make use of that or any other kind of 
thickening, but will boil down the sauce, and con- 
dense the whole amount of tomatoes and season- 
ing into a small quantity, rub it through a strainer 
and keep it ready for use in like manner with the 
Espagnole and Sauce Creole. 

USE OF STOCK SAUCES. 

In order to let the uninitiated into the knowledge 
of the reasons why these sauces are made, let u9^ 
show a few dishes. 

TRIPE A LA CREOLE. 

Cut some cooked tripe in strips, size of a little 
finger, give it a boil up in a saucepan of water; 
then drain away the water and put with the tripe 
one spoonful of sauce Creole, and two spoonfuls 
of Espagnole, shake up, make hot and serve.

STEWED KIDNEYS A LA CREOLE. 

Cut the kidneys lengthwise, then across into 
small pieceSf give them a boil in a saucepan of 
-water and skim away the top, drain off the water 
and put in with the kidneys one spoonful of sauce 
tomato and two of Espagnole and make hot 

MACARONI A LA CREOLE. 

Break the sticks of macaroni in three pieces; 
boil in salted water half an hour; drain away the 
water and pour over the macaroni equal quantities 
of sauce tomato and Espagnole, shake up and 
serve hot. 

RAGOUT OF BEEF A LA CREOLE. 

Cut some beef into pieces about the size of an 
eggt chop an onion or two and fry in oil or butter 
in a deep saucepan light brown, then put in the 
pieces of beef and without adding any water shut 
down with a tight lid and let the meat simmer in 
its own juice, and steam for an hour or longer, 
until it, too, begins to brown on the bottom, then 
stir it about and continue the browning and pre- 
sently add to it a ladleful of soup stock (bouillon) 
and a ladleful of sauce tomato. Season with salt; 
let stew at the side an hour longer. Serve in flat 
dishes with croutons of fried bread. In case there 
is no tomato sauce on hand rub the contents of a 
can of tomatoes through a strainer, add it to the 
beef instead of the sauce, put in two cloves of 
garlic and a ladleful of Espagnole and let it stew 
down thick. 

HARICOT OF MUTTON A LA CREOLE. 

Cut the mutton to be used into pieces about the 
size of an egg, or chop the breasts of mutton in 
squares and wash in cold water. Cut about the 
same quantity of carrots and turnips into shapes 
like quarters of apples, and also two large onions 
the same way. Put them all at once into a broad 
and shallow saucepan with some oil and keep them 
shaken about over the fire until all are partially 
browned, then add soup stock (bouillon) and 
lEspagnole in equal proportions and enough in all 
to cover the meat and let stew slowly at the side 
ior one or two hours. The mutton fat and oil will 
rise at one side of the saucepan and must be skim- 
med off. Serve one or two pieces of meat in each 
dish with vegetables at each end. 

BOXnLLABAISSE AND COURTBOUILLON. 

These two dishes of fish when prepared by the 
best Creole cooks are so very similar in all respects 
but one that they seem to belong one to the other. 
Bouillabaisse is the old, a dish of the ante-tomato 
period, a remainder from the middle ages when 
saffron was plentifully employed for a choice sea- 
soning. Courtbouillon is the new, a transmutation 
of the same wrought by the introduction of the 
tomato ; it is scarcely seventy-five years old. This 
accounts for the particular affection some of the 



old and gray-haired cutsiniers exhibit for the for- 
mer dish, the pertinacity with which they will 
bring it forward at every sly chance that occurs, 
with little regard to the fact that the present 
generation cares nothing for it They look back 
to a time when its production to perfection was 
among their best works, and they make it again 
and again, always surprised to find it is no longer 
an object of admiration. I have watched some 
such superannuated old cronies, had two or three 
together at times and saw them comparing qualities 
o£ t}ieir respective boxes of Spanish saffron ; seen 
tK^m hold joint sessions over the pot of tawny 
colored stew, take their bottle of sour wine with it 
and eat it all themselves, seeing they could get few 
or none to help. Still, bouillabaisse is a savory 
dish and good in some degree ; it is better, liowever^ 
with the saffron left out, and with tomatoes added 
instead, but thdn it is no longer the ancient dish 
but the new — courtbouillon, or '^fish au court- 
bouillon." At least that is how it appears to me. 
Sometimes I have surprised these old fellows 
with the query: "What is the difference between 
bouillabaisse and courtbouillon besides the differ- 
ence of color? Both look nearly alike, and they 
are stewed alike; one is a brownish yellow with 
saffron and a little curry in it, the other is a reddish 
brown with the color of the tomatoes — ^what is the 
difference otherwise?" "The difference? Oh, there 
is a great difference. In the first place bouilla- 
baisse is made with white wine, and courtbouillon 
is made with red wine ; and in the next place the 
difference is — er — well, you see, one is boo-ycr- 
base and the other is cor-boo-yon." 

There is another little difference, however, for 
by some means or other bouillabaisse has gotten 
into literature, while courtbouillon has not; for 
one instance, it is stated the elder Dumas prided 
himself upon making bouillabaisse, and, for 
another a great English novelist wrote a "Ballad 
of Bouillabaisse," which alone has been sufficient 
to raise the "what is it" question, and a magazine 
gives it out as an interesting discovery that "it is 
a sort of fish chowder." That is not far wrong, 
yet not quite right, according to the Creole 
practice. Boston cooks make bouillabaisse in the 
Boston hotels where the greatest number of public 
and private banquets are prepared. What they 
make is a curry soup with fish in it and the ordinary 
soup vegetables. But there is an exceedingly tired 
look comes into the Creole cook's face when he 
sees the Boston cooks make bouillabaisse that way 
down in his country, not only because the Creole 
prefers saffron to curry, but because he does not 
make it or serve it as soup, but as fish, with which 
the sauce is the better part It may be true that 
somewhere in a book we have read of "Bouilla* 
baisse or Provencale soup," but that makes m>

"difference while the Creoles show it to be fish 
stewed in wine and saffron, for the Creole terri- 
tory is the Provence of America ; the people have 
the old Provencal ways with new ways grafted 
upon them, and they have the earlier dish of 
•bouillabaisse made in their own way, which was 
perfection in days before tomatoes were known, 
and now in common with the newer. people with 
new ways they have the later dish of courtbouillon. 
There is another difference which is of more 
practical concern to us, which is that while the 
-dish of bouillabaisse is not in any special demand 
and is only just very well in its way and for the 
occasional gratification of an epicurean curiosity, 
the courtbouillon has come to be one of the 
standard dishes of the Southern hotels and restau- 
rants, and it is incumbent upon every cook to be 
able to produce it, and upon every steward to know 
when it is made as it ought to be. It is named in 
the printed bill of fare of every restaurant of any 
note. The hotels serve it either for breakfast, 
lunch, or dinner, some having it for a regular dish 
on the breakfast bill the same as the restaurants. 
The name is nothing new. Courtbouillon in the 
North and over the sea signifies the seasoned broth 
in which a fish is to be boiled wh61e, but this of the 
Creole cuisine is different 

BOUILLABAISSE. 

Take a large fish of any kind that can be cut in 
slices without bones, take out the back-bone and 
cut the two sides in pieces of the right size for 
serving individually. Chop the bone and head in 
pieces and put them on to boil in two quarts of 
water. That makes the stock. Put into the stock 
a good pinch of saffron. It is in threads, something 
like fine-cut tobacco — can be bought, put up in tin 
boxes — and it colors the stock yellow. Put into 
the stock, also, a couple of bay leaves, a leek or 
green onion and seasoning of salt and pepper. 

Then to cook the fish, take a broad and shallow 
hotel saucepan and pour into it about a cupful of 
oil ; mince a large onion quite fine and three or 
four cloves of garlic likewise and put them in with 
the oil. Dip the slices of fish in flour, getting a 
good coating on both sides, lay them on top of the 
minced onion in the saucepan, set over the fire, let 
simmer until they begin to color, and turn the 
slices over. At this stage in the cooking some 
will shake in a teaspoonful of curry powder, but 
the high- French, Provencal and Creole cooks will 
not Curry is an Anglo-Indian product and not 
considered to belong to such ultra Gallic dishes as 
this. Now pour into the saucepan a pint of white 
wine, and then through a strainer add the quart or 
more of saffron-colored fish stock and let the whole 
simmer gently while the half-browned flour that 
the fish was coated with thickens the liquor and 



all the oil that has been used oozes to the top to be 
thoroug^y removed by skimming. The sauce is 
thick — thicker than turtle soup. Make some toast 
very thin, brown and dry. Serve the fish upon 
the toast and the sauce poured over it But a 
Creole restaurant dish of either this or court- 
bouillon is a soup plateful with the fish upon a slice 
of toast like a ^^Boston fancy** oyster stew. 

FISH AU COURTBOUILLON. 

Take a five-pound fish of any good variety, such 
as snapper, red fish or trout, remove the backbone 
and head, and boil them in water to make the 
stock. While it is boiling throw in a dozen cloves, 
a few pieces of soup vegetables, two bay leaves, 
and add a small can of tomatoes. Next, pour a 
cupful of oil into a broad and shallow hotel sauce- 
pan ; mince a large onion and two or three cloves 
af garlic and fry in the oil until they begin to 
color. Add a basting-spoon of flour and stir it 
over the fire, then add through a strainer two or 
three pints of tomato stock, stir until it has become 
thick ; add a pint of wine. Lay in the fish cut in 
slices; season with salt and cayenne and let stew 
until the fish is sufliciently cooked, about half an 
hour, and skim off all the oil as it arises. The 
sauce should be thick. Serve the fish on thin toast 
or with a strip of toast in the dish and the sauce 
poured over. 

EXPEDIENCY. 

The dish of fish, au courtbouillon, is usually 
vei'y good as served for the hotel dinner, and only 
a very poor imitation as served for breakfast. 
This is because the orders at breakfast are few and 
do not justify a careful preparation, and something 
is apt to be improvised out ol tomato sauce and 
Espagnole that is not calculated to commend the 
dish to the customer for a second trial. Even for 
the dinner it may be diflicult to prepare it through 
want of the proper kind of saucepan. The cook 
who once knows what the dish ought to be, how- 
ever, will reflect that there are more ways to kill 
a kitten besides running a railroad train over it 
The courtbouillon can be produced very well 
indeed in a deep baking pan set in the oven on the 
bottom. Put in the oil, the onion, garlic, and slices 
of fish, then a pint or a quart — as the quantity 
needed may be — of tomatoes passed through a 
strainer, then soup-stock from the regular stock- 
boiler, enough to cover the slices of fish ; salt and 
pepper and then bake it Add thickening .and a 
cup of wine; tip up the pan and skim off all the oil 
from one end ; serve on slices of thin toast 

THE USE OF WINE IN COOKING. 

The liking for wine in cooked dishes is an 
acquired taste; people of simple habits learn to 
relish the wine- flavored viands very slowly, if ever. 



882 



THE AMERICAN COOK. 



It is only the Creoles who use it for cooking — if 
they can get it — as freely as water. But they use 
wine at their meals instead of coffee, tea or milk, 
a cheap wine which they buy by the gallon or keg, 
and often dilute it with water ; it is no dearer than 
coffee or the other common beverages. It all 
depends upon what sort of people the cooking is 
lor, whether there should be a quart of wine in 
the dish or only a quarter of a pint. 

THE USE OF OIL IN COOKING. 

The use of oil for cooking purposes is spreading 
very fast. Refined cotton-seed oil can now be 
bought at the mills at fifty cents per gallon by the 
barrel; it takes the place of butter and lard for 
frying, it is as yearly tasteless as an oil can be, and 
has the appearance of olive oil. Creole land is 
not a butter-making land, and oil has always been 
the common cooki ug and seasoning ingredient 
with them. It comes in good place to beat into 
fritter and pancake batter, is quite an improve- 
ment, but does not seem so appropriate to butter 
green peas or parsnips with. But it all depends on 
where you were raised. 

SAFFRON AND CURRY. 

A great many people who are strangers to the 
peculiarities of the Creole cuisine have met with 
saffron, but it was in the sick room, in the form of 
saffron tea, an infusion of a beautiful yellow color 
that is administered to people who are afflicted 
with jaundice and are of a beautiful yellow color, 
too, probably on the principle that like cures like ; 
but what there is in the flavor of this herb to 
commend it for an ingredient of a family stew the 
many will be at a loss to discover. There is 
nothing plainer, however, than that our tastes are 
influenced by climate and degrees of latitude, and 
it is a preservative instinct that leads to the use of 
the proper corrective. Curry, both powder and 
paste, has been persistently thrust into the English 
people's dinners by the influence of the many 
returned East Indian officers and their families 
who learn to like it in a hot climate, but it falls into 
disuse in one quarter as fast as it is advanced in 
another. Curries come to be natural enough 
dishes, however, in the South United States and 
not nearly so many people will think a chicken is 
wasted when curry powder is shaken upon it down 
here as in the North. Saffron is a flowering herb. 
Curry powder is composed of several ingredients 
of which the principal is turmeric, and that is a 
root, a kind af ginger, with nearly the same prop- 
erties as ginger; it is that which gives curry its 
vellow color. The housewives of the Southern 

• 

States very generally use turmeric to give a yellow 
color to pickled cabbage and onions. Ground 
cardamons, coriander and pepper are added to 
turmeric and we have curry powder, a good mix- 



ture of good spices, good enough to administer as 
a medicine in an attack of green apples, and oi^ht 
to be better known than it is. Still it is faiily 
appreciated everywhere along the Gulf shores,, 
and also by about six persons out of every hundred 
that we meet on the cars and in the streets. But 
there is many a Creole familj' sitting round a 
table provided with a steaming saffron stew made 
in this way. 

HACHIS A LA CREOLE. 

Cut a piece of cooked mutton or beef in thin 
slices and some potatoes the same way, then divide 
the slices into little triangular shaped pieces and 
set them all over the fire to stew, with enough of 
broth or water to barely cover ; add a leek or green 
onion cut small, a sprig of thyme, a minced pod 
of red pepper and some salt. While it is stewing, 
make some saffron tea by pouring boiling water to 
some saffron in a cup and when it has become 
strong strain it into the stew. It makes it of a 
bright yellow color, and the stew should be made 
thick by boiling down. Serve with dry toast. 

CREOLE CODFISH AND POTATOES. 

Mash cooked salt codfish and potatoes together 
in the usual way for codfish balls, but instead of 
balling it up dry, put it in a saucepan with a little 
onion very finely minced, a small amount of red 
pepper pod the same way, a spoonful of oil, a raw 
egg and a spoonful of saffron tea, and stir the 
mixture over the fire long enough for the eggs in 
it to be cooked. When turned out into the dish 
it is about the concistency of freshly mashed 
potatoes, but of a yellow butter color; it is served 
for breakfast or lunch. 



American codfish balls are an object of aversion 
to the Creole cooks, and perhaps to some others 
besides, and cause them so much annoyance that 
they never make any croquette in the round shape 
nor form any sort of compound in that fashion 
for fear by any mischance any person should for 
a moment suppose that it was a codfish ball, and 
they were the makers. 



The foregoing are the only examples I can call 
to mind of the use of potatoes in any Creole dishes. 
They make meat stews with squashes cut up in 
them and egg-plants, cucumbers, okra, green peas 
and string beans, but rarely use potatoes. 



GUMBO. 

Now we are going to plow in new ground, as 
many a new settler in Florida has a chance to do, 
for it does not appear that anybody has ever 
observed that the queer little Indians, mostly 
women and girls who sit on their heels on the 
ground at the market corners, almost as passive 



THE AMBBIOAN COOK. 



883 



and motionless as wooden figures are the undis- 
puted monopolists of gumbo. I wonder how many 
or few, if any, of the thousands of visitors from 
-distant scenes and divers customs ever unravel the 
enigma of their being there at all. They stop to 
look, sometimes, these plumed and beautified units 
of the superior race and the little black Indian 
■eyes look up expectantly. 

"What is that?" says the gloved finger, pointing 
to a lot of dull green powder in a sack with the 
top rolled down. 

"Gumbo file,'' says the small voice that belongs 
to the little black eyes ; so small that a whisper 
<:ould be heard better. 

"And what is that?" says the hand with the 
glistening bracelet, pointing to some bunches of 
dried leaves. 

"Lau-rel," (bay leaves) replies swarthy face, 
looking around at the two or three other swarthy 
faces as if asking what they think of the fine birds 
who ask questions but never buy. 

"And what is that?" says the gayf ringed parasol 
which all the swarthy faces are admiring, as it 
points to a pile of pieces of pliable matted stuff 
that looks like it might be the roots of swamp 
rushes. 

But the little swarthy faces do not answer, they 
only turn to each other in perplexity; they can 
look and speak but never smile, and they cannot 
«ay "scrubbing brushes" to these shining silk 
dresses — what would they know about scrubbing 
brushes? 

"But I must ask what this is,^* says the bonnet 
with the bright bird upon it, nodding at some 
bundles of red roots. 

"Oh, come along, I know what that is," answers 
the youth with the cane, who has been all this 
while watching for signs of life in the large eyed 
Indian girl in the back-ground — "come along, 
that's sassafras root, and this other stuff is either 
slippery elm or something like it." 

And they passed on, knowing no more about 
the gumbo monopoly than they did at first. The 
market master comes around next to collect his 
taxes, and all the other venders have something to 
say, either to complain of small sales or high 
•charges, or to joke philanthropically about a 
burdensome taxation, but when he comes to the 
gumbo sellers the little black eyes are raised to his, 
the swarthy hands drop the ready coin into his 
palm, he passes on and never a word is spoken. 
So everything about gumbo is enigmatical. It is 
a strange little stock of merchandise to expect to 
sell, and strange to say they never seem to sell 
anything, and always their stock looks the same 
with the same little horn measure in the same bag 
of green dust every day, but certainly there must 
he buyers who come out of the Creole quarters 



early In the morning and buy, else there soon 
would be a failure of coins for the tax-gatherer. 
And there is a peculiarly thick mystery about the 
stuff itself. Where is there a gumbo factory? 
There are many cities of a hundred thousand 
people that do not and never did contain an ounce 
of gumbo, yet some have heard of such a thing as 
gumbo soup. But suppose they should want 
gumbo, how would they get it? Only the passive 
Indian women sitting on the market corners 
prepare it and sell it, and what will the city people 
with their fast mail and immediate delivery, and 
through express and rapid messengers do if the 
Indian women has just tied up the mouth of her 
sack and disappeared in the depths of the forest 
primeval and all the gumbo with her? 

Gumbo file (felay-dry) is supposed to be okra 
dried and powdered, but that is only a supposition, 
and it is' still unkown whether the modern arts of 
adulteration have reached the Indian manufac- 
turers or not, and whether the adulterant, if used, 
is not better than the real article. Gumbo file is a 
native American product, yet remains unprotected ; 
the government chemist knows nothing about it, 
there has been no quantitative analysis of the 
several brands of Seminole, Teche, and Cherokee. 
It is a Southern agricultural resource, yet the 
patriotic patent office reports do not report it, and 
our consuls to foreign states are taking no steps to 
extend the market for it or present the claims of 
gumbo soup. 

But at some time in the future, when the last of 
these groups of moccasined Indian women with 
the last sacks af gumbo file upon their backs shall 
have disappeared in the depths of the virgin forest 
for the last time an interesting inquiry will be 
raised of what permanent effect upon the dietary 
practices of these Southern States, their three 
hundred years of sitting on market corners, pre- 
senting their singular wares for sale has had. Who 
taught us to make and like lye hominy ? Indians. 
Who popularized "sassafrax," sarsaparilly, winter* 
green, slippery elm bark, chewing roots, chewing 
gum, corn cob pipes, tobacco? And who implanted 
in the nature of their sociable neighbors the French 
immigrants and their descendants the Creoles a 
love for chicken gumbo soup? 

CREOLE SOUP. 

It is characteristic of the Creole soups that they 
are always thick, fit to serve for a complete meal, 
both meat and drink. The Creoles are in accord 
with the north frigid people hi thinking the trans- 
parent French consommes are transparently too 
thin and next akin to nothingness. A good example 
of a Creole soup is a dish of Coney Island Clam 
chowder when the clams are numerous enough to 
crowd out the potatoes. Their soups are like the

THE AMERICAN COOK. 



Spanish olla-podrida and the Turkish pillaws and 
such stews as the lights of harems are said to feed 
each other with, using their fingers instead of 
forks, a proceeding which would, however, have 
its inconveniences if chicken gumbo were the dish 
served because of its mucilaginous adhesive quality 
and the linked sweetness long drawn out which 
still connects each departing morsel with the 
parent dish. It is this mucilaginous quality, 
resembling that of cold flaxseed tea or infusion of 
slippery elm bark, that is the recommendation 
both of the green and okra pods and of gumbo 
file, and the more slipperyelmical the soup can be 
made by boiling down, the better it will be. 

Regarding these articles on Creole cookery as 
simple studies of 

OTHER people's WAYS, 

without any intention of ridiculing them, there 
may be nothing amiss in my giving one of my 
sources of authority for some of these glutinous 
statements. He was a Creole-French captain of 
a steamboat in the Bayou Lafourche trade, an 
epicure among the Creoles, a fractious man, and 
one, it was said, to be impossible to suit with a 
cook. The boys who owned the earth down 
there let me have that boat because I was a 
stranger ; and they watched, expecting to see that 
choleric captain chase me over the gang-plank to 
the shore just before the boat pulled out, but 
nothing of the sort occurred. I got along well ; 
cooked for him and his few French passengers 
during the whole five months of the sugar season, 
and he paid me well. It was a simple operation; 
he only wanted to show a cook how it should be 
done, and then have it done his way, and I let 
him. Twice a day, among other things, he had a 
saucepan of green okra simmered down in olive 
oil to that condition that when he took a pod on 
his fork the gum that roped from it like the fila- 
ments of thick molasses had to be wound around 
it with his knife before he could get it clear into 
his mouth; and he tried the quality of all the 
gumbo soups by the same sort of long measure. 



CHICKEN GUMBO SOUP. 

Cut two chickens raw into joints and chop 
these smaller, making about twenty pieces of each 
xowi. Put some buiter ^ oil in a broad saucepan, 
a large onion minced small, & couple of slices of 
ham cut small and then the chicken, pu^ on the 
lid and then set over the fire to braise in its or'n 
steam. Watch that it does not burn, but let ths 
pieces aquire a light brown color with frequent 
shaking about. Then put in either a cupful of 
gumbo file or five or six times as much green 
okra sliced thin and fill up with four quarts ot 
soup stock or broth. Tie up a sc«al{ bwnct o£ 



soup herbs, consisting of parsley, green marjoram^ 
bay leaves, leek and celery, and let them boil in 
the soup. Simmer at the side of the range an 
hour or two, 6kim off the fat that rises, season witiv 
salt and pepper and take out the faggot of herbs. 
It makes a dull greenish soup. Although there 
is no particular rule about the quantity of gumbo- 
to use, it is expected there will be enough to make 
the soup thick without the use of flour. 

Boil some rice in plenty of water, wash it in 
cold water when done and pour it into a sieve to 
drain. Serve a good spoonful of the loose rice- 
grains in each plate of soup as it is sent in. 



TOMATO GUMBO. 

It is best made with g^reen okra. Commence 
the same way as in making Spanish sauce, by 
frying together some oil, ham, onions, garlic, car- 
rots and turnips. Add soup stock and boil, %vith 
a foggot of soup herbs in it about an hour. Strain 
into another saucepan, add equal quantities of to- 
matoes and green okra and simmer until the okra 
is nearly all dissolved, it may be two hours. Skim, 
season, serve with rice. The tomatoes should not 
be strained into this soup, but cut into pieces. 
The soup should be thick enough with okra with- 
out the use of flour. 



CRAB GUMBO. 

Chop some crabs, already cooked, into large 
pieces, the body making four or six and each 
large claw two pieces ; crack the shells with the 
back of a knife. Make the same way as chicken 
gumbo, using the pieces cf crab in place of chicken, 
or, make a strong vegetable soup thick with 
gumbo file and boil the pieces of crab in it 
Serve with rice in the plates. 

A dan tier sort of crab gumbo could be and 
doubtless is in some places made with the crab- 
meat picked from the shells. The above method 
of chopping in. pieces and serving the shells and 
all in the plates is the only way it ever appears at 

public tables.

Shrimps and Oysters. 

Of the following ways of serving the shrimp, 
it may be said that the broil and the brochette 
(skewer) are acceptable to nearly all people at any 
meal though best for breakfast. The others are 
more easily disposed of for lunch than for dinner. 
And it should not be forgotten that shrimp are 
good enough served plain with the common cruet 
seasonings. 

SHRIMP GUMBO. 

Wash a gallon of shrimps in plenty of water to 
free them from sand and boil them in six quarts- 
o£ wflL^^r, with » oiece of bacon, two leeks and twa

bajrleaves. Take off the scum that rises. In an- 
other saucepan fry a minced onion and two or 
three cloves of garlic in oil, put in a quart of to- 
matoes, only cut in pieces, and a cupful of gumbo 
file (which is powder of dried okra) and then add 
the shrimp liquor through a fine strainer. Let 
boil together. Cool the shrimps, shell them, cut 
in pieces and throw them into the soup. Salt and 
red pepper to taste. All Creole soups are thick 
and nearly all eaten with rice. Have some cooked 
rice ready and serve a spoonful in each plate of 
gumbo as dished up. 



LYONAISB OF SHRIMP, A LA CREOLB. 

Take about equal quantities of onions cut small, 
tomatoes, and shrimp cooked and hulled. Fry 
the onions, put in the tomatoes and let cook to- 
gether ; add the shrimp and stir up with a few 
spoonfuls of espagnole sauce and salt and pepper. 
It is a thick mixture ; serve it heaped in a flat dish 
with ornamental strips of toast 



SHRIMP SALAD. 

Cut up the shrimps and prepare about an equal 
measure of minced white lettuce, endive or celery, 
according to the season. Put them into a bowl 
together. Add a small amount of onion finely 
minced and about four times a& much parsley. 
Put in then a good spoonful of pepper relish or 
hot sauce, which is the same as minced piccalilly 
or minced pickled peppers, etc. Then olive oil 
enough to moisten the salad and a like amount of 
vinegar and stir together till well mixed. 



SHRIMP WITH RISOTTO. 

'/his is a good example of a Creole dish, and of 
Creole frugality in their domestic cooking ; it is 
shrimp and rice, the shrimp cooked first and taken 
up, and the rice cooked in and flavored by the 
same water. 

Wash a quart or more of fresh, uncooked 
shrimp in plenty of water to free them from sand ; 
then drop them into boiling water, enough to 
cover; put in two bay leaves, a spoonful salt, and 
same of minced onion, and a piece of red pepper- 
pod, and boil about half an hour ; then strain the 
water into another saucepan, and to a quart of it 
allow a heaping cup of rice, and cook with a lid 
on till done. The shrimps, which are nearly 
white and transparent while raw, turn light red 
in cooking, and they give color enough to the 
rice without other addition; but to serve at a 
Creole table it will be necessary to add saffron 
infusion enough to make it yellow. Shape the 
rice by pressing into cups, and turn out a shape 
into a dish. Remove the shells from the shrimps ; 
moisten with a spoonful of the liquor, reserved I 



for the purpose ; serve them placed around the 
shape of rice. Risotto is an Italian word. Curry 
powder can be used in place of saffron. 



SHRIMP A LA BORUELAISE. 

Boil the shrimps in water with seasonings, as 
above stated, when done strain off the liquor and 
boil it down ; to half a cupful of it add a cupful 
of espagnole sauce, and half as much claret and a 
pinch of cayenne ; put in the picked shrimps and 
stew together. Serve with croutons of bread 
fried in oil. 



SHRIMP SUR LE GRILL. 

Broiled shrimp. Take the largest already cooked 
and divested of their shells; dredge with salt and 
black pepper; put them in the wire oyster broiler; 
brush both sides with oil ; broil quickly, and serve 
on toast with quartered lemon. 



SHRIMP A LA BROCHETTE. 

String the shrimps already cooked and hulled, 
on iron skewers, and cut straight down to make 
them of even size; dip in beaten egg on a plate, 
and then in cracker dust, and fry in hot oil. Serve 
on thin toast, witlidrawing the skewer as they are 
served. 



OYSTERS A LA BROCHETTE. 

Run a dozen or more of oysters on a skewer^ 
drop them into the frying kettle of hot oil or lard 
for three or four minutes to shrink them, then 
finish by broiling on the gridrion over hot coals* 

Serve on buttered toast, withdrawing the 
skewer, and garnish with lemon and parsley. 



OYSTER SOUP A LA CREOLE. 

or, a«flf pnes herbesy or New Orleans style. For 
a hundred persons: 2 gallons of oysters and 
their liquor, 2 gallons of milk, 2 cans of mush- 
rooms, I onion, a handful of parsley, and half a 
pound of butter. 

Set the oysters over the fire to scald but not 
boil, when shrunken pour into a colander to drain^ 
and set the liquor on again to boil. Boil the milk 
separately. Mince onion, parsley and mushrooms 
fine and throw them in a large saucepan with the 
butter, add the oyster liquor through a fine 
strainer, then the milk, and thicken till like cream 
with flour mixed with milk. Season with salt 
and pepper and put in the oysters at last.

The most vicious looking halfbushel that can 
be found in a Southern market is the basket of

salt water crayfish, which has a place at many of 
the turtle stalls or frog butchers places of business; 
they are evidently a not very plentiful commodity, 
for the proportion in sight compared with such 
other things as crabs and shrimp, is very small, 
and yet is equal to the demand, as the price asked 
for them proves. They look as little like anything 
good to eat as possible, more like noisome insects 
to be avoided. They are in color a mixture of 
bloodred and black, shaped like a lobster, though 
no larger than a Southern shrimp, but they refuse 
to double themselves up as the lobster and shrimp 
do, but spread out both claws and tail in an atti- 
tude of eternal obstreperousness. That is why 
the dealers always handle them either with tongs 
or claw-hammers. Perhaps they look better to 
some people, but the truth is the crayfish is some- 
thing of a thorn in the side of American cookery, 
and has caused many of us who are interested in 
it too much annoyance to allow any care for its 
merits. The Creoles use them, and by some they 
are thought to be peculiar to Creole cookery, 
-which, however, is not the case; the custom in 
this regard being but a continuance of French 
customs, for of all the ornamental dishes of fish 
described in French culinary works, one-half at 
least would require of us to produce them accord- 
ing to rule, to use crayfish in one form or another; 
crayfish tails and button mushrooms; crayfish 
tails, and oysters; crayfish shells pounded in a 
mortar and mixed in a sauce ; crayfish pounded 
and worked into butter to make crayfish butter; a 
fine crayfish on a skewer; a ragout of crayfish 
and mussels — these and forty other cries of cray- 
fish, crayfish, where there are no crayfish, and 
where nobody could be bribed or coaxed into eat- 
ing a crayfish if there were cause enough for us to 
take the ground that crayfish are. not much ac- 
count anyhow.

for the stew with tomatoes. 



AT THE FROG BUTCHERS. 

P'rogs' hind legs, and small one-ounce frogs at 
that, may please the Canadians well enough, but 
at the Creole markets one may see frogs as large 
as sqirrels, skinned and hung up in pairs by the 
fore feet, while at the same time live frogs of the 
same lot are on the same stalls in cages, of such 
huge proportions that I think a guess of their live 
weight at two pounds each is likelier to be below 
the true weight of some of them than above it. 
The market price of a pair of such frogs skinned, 
white, broad backed and entire except the heads, 
is from fifty csnts on a cool morning down to 
thirtv cents at the close of market hours on a 
warm day. As there would be no supply if there 
>vas no demand, it is a certainty that these are 
sought after by some people. As to the methods 
of cooking, it is sufficient to know that any way 



SNAILS. 



A few snails are offered, with tickets announc- 
ing that they are newly imported from France. 
The snailshells are closed with glue by the snail 
itbelf at a certain time of the year and the whole 
lot looks as dry as a box of nuts. Snails are used 
to make a broth that is supposed to be beneficial 
in cases of consumption. 



CREOLE SWEET TOMATOES. 

All over the South stewed tomatoes, instead of 
being seasoned with salt and pepper, are made 
sweet with sugar, and when boiled down to what 
is in effect a tomato preserve, it is called Creole 
Sauce. Sweetened tomatoes and sweetened sweet 
potatoes are Southern specialties, whether of 
Creole origin or not, which Northerners first 
endure, then admire, then embrace. 



CREOLE BREAD AND PASTRY. 

There is very little in this line that is peculiar, 
the Creole shops and showcases being stocked 
with the same French and Italian bread and 
cakes that may be found in any other part of the 
country. They call almost any kind of bun or 
roll a brioche^ according to common French usage, 
and have a very popular "wine cake," which is a 
bright, yeast-raised sweet roll baked in a patty 
pan and dipped while hot into a syrup of sug^r 
and wine, which partially drives upon the cake. 
This and the cream pie or Hourte do frangifan^'* 
of every Creole bakery those who have ever 
studied the subjeet may find pleasure in recogniz- 
ing as the unchanged descendants of the line that 
received the royal sanction of Louis XIV and 
Lous X V ; of Compiegne, Trinanan and Versailes. 



There is one kind of banana so handsome 
that it is not even good to eat raw — that is to say 
it is not so good as the fat looking, yellow sort. 
But it has a purple flush that talks to you about 
the tropics and it acts as a snare to draw your 
dime and then goes back on you in the matter of 
flavor. It is too much to expect of the banana 
that it should be red as a rose, perfumed and sweet 
and good to eat all at once, and when you have 
tried all you settle down with the knowledge 
that the variety known as Havana lady-fingers 
are the best flavored. They are only of the com- 
mon pale, yellow color, and are small, as the 
name implies, but are all the better for the hotel 
table on that account, as it does not tire the guests 
so much to carry them to their rooms after din* 



THE AMERICAN COOK. 



387 



ner as some of the ponderous varieties would do. 
The flavor of the banana is very much dependent 
upon the way it is ripened by the seller, for the 
bunches are always gathered and shipped in a 
green. state, and while the foolish dealers, in cold 
weather keep them cold till they turn black and 
spoil without ripening at all, the wise ones hang 
them in a dark room built like an ice-chest, with 
double walls, and keep up such a degree of heat 
as bananas are used to down in the land where 
the monkeys chatter, and they swell and ripen, 
turn to a rich and shining yellow^ and become 
all odoriferousness and sact:harosity, and sell at 
five cents apiece in a few days. 



The Florida Tnrtle. 

The Florida turtle knows where the frost line 
is, it is on a level with the surface of the deep 
blue sea, and he lives below it during the short 
period of midwinter, but at this dawning of the 
early Florida spring he may be seen on every 
side, even on the market stalls divested of his 
head, of half his shell, of all his skin and still alive 
or at any rate moving and therefore not dead, a 
striking instance of the healthfulness of the Flor- 
ida climate. To dress a turtle the cooks always 
first cut off the head and then immerse the turtle 
in boiling water preparatory to removing shell 
and skin ; the dealers, however, in the places where 
turtles abound, operate entirely with a knife, cut 
the meat out of the shell and display and sell it 
by the pound like any other meat. This method 
does not destroy the muscular motion for hours 
after the turtle is killed, but the scalding process 
is instant death, therefore, we shall not change 
our method, although the turtle meat on the 
stalls does look as clean as lean pork and the 
turtle eggs are piled high around it. 

The turtle is one of the elements of every 
Imaginary picture of Florida; coming in out of 
the wet to bask on a coral key in the semi-tropical 
sunshine, he is almost as much a representative 
sign for the Southern land*s end as the white bear 
is of the arctic region, he is the link in the chain 
of connection with the West Indies, with South 
America and with semi-tropical Australia, all of 
which are made dear to the imaginations of 
European and particularly of English gourmands 
by their production of this most esteemed of table 
luxuries. Visitors to Florida ought to make a 
good aquaintance with the turtle on his native 
sands and again at the cheerful table and try to 
realize why such huge sums have been spent in 
procuring it for every civic feast that has taken 
place over there, thousands of miles from its 
native coral beds for hundreds of years past, for 
••things are not what they seem" to the eye but 



rise in importance according to mental associa- 
tions. 

The turtle loses its great recommendation to 
the rich, and exclusive of extreme dearness in 
this country, for the price is low enough to put 
this kind of meat within the means of a vast 
number — more than know how to use it, or than 
even know the name and nature of turtle. A 
clipping of light reading went the rounds recently, 
referring to the price of turtle, that read crudely 
enough to have come from England, where turtle 
is really dear unless they use "sun-dried turtle 
from Australia," as their papers advertise it, which 
bears a low price. This New York clipping re- 
ferred to turtle soup being charged for at some 
restaurants only ten cents a plate higher than 
mock turtle, and thought it evidence of fraud that 
turtle soup was offered so cheap. The common 
fact is, that the best brand of canned turtle, of 
first quality of meat, sold in the cans, with 
piope-ly assorted meat in each can — for there are 
severa. kinds of meat in a turtle — a;id a fair pro- 
portion of the famous green fat in each, can be 
bought by the case at twenty-five cents per two- 
pound can, about fifteen cents per pound for meat 
ready dressed, and without bone or waste. If 4i 
pound would only make a quart of soup, the **only 
ten cents more" would be good pay for tlie differ- 
ence. But turtle is only one ingredient out of several 
in turtle soup, and when the mock turtle is made, 
which has everything else except the turtle in it, 
a can of turtle cut in pieces, for about three 
quarts will be found to make it as rich as the 
most critical London alderman could desire, and 
with meat enough and green fat for every plate. 

Probably there has never been a recipe given 
for making turtle soup that did not call for 
wine to be added at the finish, and that one re- 
quirement has prevented turtle from coming into 
frequent use, for it is not only that very few 
people like the wine flavor in meat or fish, at first, 
but wine is associated with ideas of expense, with 
the thought of a dollar bottle of wine being re- 
quired to make a twenty -five cent bowl of soup, 
and while this impression prevails some other 
viands will continue to be chosen and the turtle 
sells slower than any other goods on the mer- 
chant's shelves. The idea is erroneous, however, 
the addition of wine is optional, like any other 
flavoring. Tlie London aldermen may empty 
into the tureen of turtle soup a bottle or two of 
the oldest and best Maderia obtainable, but that 
does not prove that the soup is not good enough 
without it. The addition that is better than wine 
and more to the popular taste, is strained tomatoes. 
With tomatoes wine is not needed and as that gets 
to be commonly understood, turtle as food will be- 
come more popular and the demand upon the 



THB AMFTKifCAN OOOK. 



packers will increase. The English markets are 
rcgularljr supplied with live turtles from the West 
Indies, only large sizes being considered worth 
canying to such a distance. There are other ways 
of cooking turtle besides in soup, and at the great 
banquets a nun jer of dishes are made of the dif- 
ferent parts, one of them being turtle dressed in 
cream sauce like our terrapin^ Maryland style; 
another way is a brown f ricasee like terrain a la 
Prascati; the liver and heart are stewed in wine, 
and the fins make a separate dish, while, for an 
effort at the ornamental, the custom is to serve 
either soup or stew in the deep shell of the turtle, 
the rim being bordered with paste baked on it and 
decorated. 

It is not every variety of turtle that has a shell 
adapted for that purpose, for of the dozen or more 
different kinds that are sold in the markets, some 
have shells as round and shallow as an umbrella, 
and others carry a covering as steep and angular 
as a mansard roof. 



TURTLE SOUP A LA CREOLE. 

Make Spanish sauce as already directed with 
considerable tomatoes in it. Take as much soup 
stock as you have sauce and mix them together, 
add salt and pepper to taste and add some thicken- 
ing when it boils. Then into every gallon put a 
can|or more of cut turtle, half a lemon cut small, 
one quarter cup of sherry. Egg balls can be made 
by pounding hard boiled yolks of eggs with a little 
butter and flour to make a yellow paste that can 
be rolled into bales and boiled in water and then 
dipped into the soup. The turtle that is bought 
alive generally furnishes turtle eggs enough with- 
out resorting to the imitation.

THE STAR BOARDER. 



A Hotel Chers Yarn. 



BY JESSUP WHITEHEAD. 

•♦The toughest customer I ever had to serve a 
steak to, was a star boarder we had down at 
Hallelujah Falls, in Tennessee,'* said a friend of 
mine, a hotel chef, as we sat at a table in a Chicago 
wine cellar. 

"Could'nt be suited any way you could fix him, 
eh?" 

"No, it was'nt just that, but he would*nt take a 
steak at all and I made up my mind he should, 
and I played him on it all summer; but he was a 
mighty fine man you must remember. I would'nt 
monkey with many men that way." 

**But what for?" I asked him, "why should you 
want to make him eat bee&teaks against his 
liking?" 



" See here " returned the chef, his eyes glisten- 
ing with merriment, "when you've got a blood as 
tony as they make 'em, one that you know loves 
good living, and when he as good as tells you that 
he loves a good beefsteak better than anything 
else, and then tells you straight up and down that 
he is not going to eat any beefsteak whatever that 
you can send him, aint you going to do your level 
best to make him ? " 

"Why of course, that is if I can without offend- 
ing him, but I don't understand.*' 

" He was a mighty fine man mind you. I met 
him down at the falls the second day aft^r he 
came there ; we were both out looking around 
there to sort of get the points about the country, 
I reckon, aud I told him straight off that I was 
the chef for this season and was going to do the 
best I could for him and 1 hoped he was going to 
stay awhile." 

" *Well,' " says he *I think you do very well in- 
deed here ; it's as good as I want' says he, *and you 
need not take any extra trouble for me/ He was 
a mighty fine man you could tell by his talk ; he 
was a big Washington lawyer and a judge and he 
had a whole mint of trustee business or something 
else all over the South besides, and I answered 
him we could do about as well as any other house 
he could think of, all except the beef, and I did 
feel a little doubtful about getting good short loins 
in that forgotten country. 

"*I know this country well,' said the judge, 
*and you will not get any good beef, at least not 
often, but when you do get hold of a sten^ a little 
better than usual, I wish you would send it to the 
two ladies sitting at my table, for they seem to be 
invalids and have very little appetite.' 

"And not to you too?" I said. 

"'Its no use,' said he, *I never take a beefsteak 
after I leave Washington City.' 

"Never?" 

"*Never. I live in hotels all the time; my 
business takes me tq all parts of the South, to 
stay in some places for weeks, but I make it a rule 
to never take a beefsteak outside of Washington. 

"'What's the reason for that?' said I. 

" 'Because there are no beefsteaks fit to eat in 
the South,' said he. 

"But suppose you are in a place where they 
should be good ? 

"Said he, 'It is impossible, it's no use, you might 
cook them well, but you have not the beef. I 
know this country like my own home — there i& 
no beef fit to eat — the hotel people don't know 
how to cut i^ — they don't know one part from an- 
other — they don't know any civilized way to cook 
and serve it. But I find abundance of other things 
to eat,* the judge went on, laughing. 'We won't 
trouble about the beefsteak.' And you bet he did 



THE AMERICAN COOK. 



38& 



find plenty other things to eat. I didn^t feel very 
good about what he said, but then nobody could 
have made me believe he was in earnest." 

**That afternoon the farmer that was going to 
supply us with beef, brought in the first animal — 
the whole carcass, mind you, and it was a terror 
sure enough, weighed just 135 pounds. 

"What do you call that thing?" says I, «*a 
Southern calf?" 

** ♦That,' says he, *is a good young steer.' " 

"Well, sir, I talked to that man till he was 
ashamed, and like to have cried, and he said the 
next one should be a good one, way up. We had 
a piece of half-civilized beef on ' hand that they 
had brought over in the buggy, and I broiled a 
steak off that for the star boarder's supper, and 
sent it in, and, sure enough, he never touched it. 
I didn't feel very good over that, but it appeared 
he never ate any hot meat for supper anyhow ; 
but he ate fish, cold ham, eggs, rolls, muffins, 
waffles, strawberries and sweet cakes, and drank 
tea and milk, and I thought he could easily be 
made to take a steak in the morning." 

"We had three ways of cooking steaks in that 
house and kept them all going on at once ; the 
regular broil over hot coals; the Texas grill — that's 
slap a steak down flat on the top of the hot range, 
you know, and let it sizzle and smoke — and the 
other way was fry in a pan with gravy. I heard 
that some of them said they could eat steak all 
day when it was fried in a pan with gravy ; and 
some of them liked the Texas grill ; but the star 
boarder, I was sure, would not look at a steak any 
way but city broiled, over a bed of clear coals, 
and I took out the l^st loin cut that was left, 
cooked it 'right and seasoned, but only plain, be- 
cause I wasn't' thinking much about the star 
boarder just yet, and sent it in hot for his break- 
fast. But it's a fact, that man never even looked 
at it, but there it set; and he ate raspberries, mut- 
ton chops, ham, eggs, bacon, liver, fish, corn 
bread, rolls, and batter cakes, and drank coffee and 
milk. I didn't feel very good over that and got 
to studying about the star boarder all day, but I 
couldn't lay for him till some better beef came in." 

"The next mountain runt that the rascally far- 
mer brought in weighed only 175 pounds, the 
whole carcass. Why, you know a good young 
steer, a three-year old, will dress out 400 pounds, 
100 pounds a quarter, 50 pounds a loin, and here 
was one less than half that weight I let on like 
wanting to lick the farmer for bringing such moun- 
tain climbers as those to me, and he promised to 
bring a splendid animal next time, the finest in 
the whole heard.'* 

"A tenderloin of this beef was not much larger 
than a hog's, but I cut long and set the piece on 
«nd and flattened to a very fidr likeness of a good 



fliet steak, broiled it and surrounded it with pota- 
toes cut Francaise and fried, garnished it, and 
thought the star boarder would take a tumble to- 
it when it was set before him. He took just sa 
much notice of it that he sent out for a separate 
dish of that kind of potatoes and would not take 
them off the steak, but he ate early peaches, 
broiled mackerel, lamb chops, fried salt pork, an 
omelet, corn muffins, buttered toast, rice cakes 
and drank chocolate and milk. Well, I didn't 
feel very good over that, but I had formed a reso- 
lution, too, by this time, and was bound to make 
him break his pledge before I got through with 
him ; only it would not do to send a thing he did 
not order, very often, only like an accidental diop» 
as if the waiter had made the mistake But he 
liked tomato sauce and I tried him with another 
filet a la sauce tomate. He didn't bite at that bait 
either, but ate tripe with tomato sauce and sliced 
tomatoes, broiled chicken, ham and eggs, and all 
the etceteras as usual. He was a tough one, but 
I had not tried much yet." 

"Well, when that candidate for the poorhouse 
brought in the fine, fat steer, as he called it, that 
he had been blowing about, what do you gv\ess it 
weighed? It weighed just 200, the whole carcass,, 
and he thought it was immense. If anybody had 
driven a thousand-pound ox into that country, the 
people would all have thought it a circus and 
quit work to go and see it And it was not only 
the small size of their cattle, but grass was scarca- 
and the critters had to eat weeds that made the 
beef taste bitter. However, I hung it to season 
and did the best I could with it, and then took 
another whack at the star boarder. I cut him a 
porterhouse steak this time, the best that could be 
got out of that beef, close to the hip bone where 
the tenderloin part of it was big, the kind of steak 
with a bone in it that our restaurant bloods call 
for, and it took a dish big enough to make the 
judge take notice of it. I didn't care if he did 
take it as a compliment if I could only have it to 
say I made him break his New Year's resolutions, 
about not eating a beefsteak outside of Washing- 
ton. I sent it to him a la Colbert; rubbed the 
dish with garlic, had some largest sized potato* 
balls, scooped out and boiled, and then light fried^ 
broiled the steak right and laid it in, put the pota* 
toes around, had some steak gravy and butter 
mixed and poured under, and let it go. Well sir, 
that shook him up, and one minute I thought 1 
had him, he ate all the potatoes out of the dish 
and fooled with the gravy hut when he left the 
table there was the steak yet, as whole as it wa& 
when I sent it in, and he went away laughing. I 
didn't feel very good over that, only^ every man 
has got a weak spot to hit if you can find where it 
is, and the next morning I would try another way.

"What meat doth this our Csesar feed on in

bluegrass quarters? You can see he knew this

spread the maitre d* hotel butter on top. touched it 
off with the last parsley there was in that house's 
garden, and a quartered lemon, and got that down

from Washington didn't know good beef the 
minute he saw it? Of course, he did. But do 
you think he ate it? No, sir; he did not. But it 
made iis resolution mighty sick, however. He 
wanted to eat that steak worse than anything I 
ever saw, but he was too starchy ; he ate all the 
potatoes, and just took and drained all the sauce 
and gravy out of the dish all over them in his 
plate, and then pushed the steak away like it was 



no good, and he turned to and ate fried chicken, 
broiled ham, shirred eggs, sally lunn, Graham 
toast and grapes, and drank coffee and milk. 
That's were the trouble was, after all. There waR 
too much to eat What does a man want of a 
little black, stringly steak, anyhow, when there is 
everything else you can name all ready for hina 
to eat instead? says I, and I got ready to cut oft . 
his grub for the next try, for I was dead in eamesi 
that he had to knock under, now that we had tbo 
beef." 

"We had a mighty pretty little white girl for a 
pantry girl in that house. Say — did you eve:; 
take notice what a difference it makes about 
boarders not kicking on the steaks when you have 
girls to wait on 'em ? It does. If you have the 
right sort of girl when some young fellow that 
don't feel very good that morning says, 'here, take 
this steak away and get me a better one, it's 
tough, I can't eat that,' that girl don't want the 
trouble of going to the kitchen again and she just 
takes up the dish and looks at it, and then looks 
at him, and says she, 'ain't you just a little bit 
cross this morning?' and she slides the steak in 
front of him again, and nine times out of ten he 
takes the steak and eats it without another word. 
We had black boys waiting on the table but they 
were no waiters. Some of them would unload a 
tray of dishes before a man just like shoveling 
com to a horse and another would swing every 
dish back and forth like a pigeon trying to alight 
and make a man bob his head forty ways for fear 
of getting hit before it got down to the table — do 
you ever read anything?" 

"Yes, sometimes " 

"That's what put me on to it Out in these 
country places you have to read ; there's nowhere 
to go and I had picked up a Cooper novel, the 
"Midshipman" where it remarked that a cup of 
coffee posseses a finer aroma when served by a 
feminine hand and it's so ; it's like old Saint An- 
thony sitting and reading his good book, you 
know, and all the demons came buzzing him and 
chinning him and couldn't make him raise his 
eyes, but the minute a woman came waltzing in 
he raised square up to get a good look at her. 
And now if I let the chicken dropoff the bill, and 
cut the mutton chops off the neck and gave 'em a 
Texas grill so the judge couldn't eat them, and 
cut the ham with no fat on it, then if I fixed him 
up a fine porter house steak worth a dollar and a 
half and had a sweet-home kind of a g^irl to wait 
on him with lace around her wrists, my high- 
toner from Washington would have to eat his 
steak, there was no way for him to get out of it 
— ain't that the way it looks to you?" 

"Yes." 

"I told you he was a tough customer, but he

I went to work and got out the finest kind of a

come in to breakfast and behold! he had left — his 
time that he came to stay was out and he went 
away on the early coach — he was gone.'* 



Two Thousand Five Hundred 

Guests. 



"Talk about pleasure resort business," said he, 
**ril tell you where I got all I wanted to do, and 
that was where we had twenty-five hundred 
guests to feed for weeks at a time, and we attended 
to them just as well, and treated them in just as 
good style, as they do where there's only a 
hundred or two." 

"Where was that?" 

"Up here at the Springs, in Virginia." 

"And you were the head cook?" 

"I was, and had what you might call seven 
other head cooks under me, and let me tell you 
what I've done. I've fed eighteen hundred people 
in three-quarters of an hour, and done it meal 
after meal, and that's what very few men can say, 
but you can just bet if ever any man was glad 
when a season was over, it was me ; it like to have 
killed me." 

"But you got good pay there .^" 

"I had two hundred a month; yes, that was 
good, but it was worth every cent of it. Why, 
we would sometimes have as many as five hund- 
red arrivals after eight o'clock at nighty and they 
all had to get supper, you know." 

"You won't have such a rush at your Florida 
job if you get it," I said, for he had in his hand 
a polite reply he had just received from a great 
hotel-keeper, telling him to send on a description 
of himself, and he laughed as he pulled at his 
gray moustache and answered: 

"I guess about five or six hundred will be crowd 
enough for me now. I'm about eighteen years 
older than I was then ; but didn't we see times, 
though, in '68 and '69.?'" 

"What sort of a range did you work on ? Was 
it set in brick, or did you use the old-fashioned 
sheet-iron ranges, and several of them?" 



**No, it wasn't that kind at all, but one solid 
ptate of iron the whole length of the kitchen, 
with fire holes under where we could put in nine- 
foot wood. It was marked o£f into seven ranges^ 
as we called them, and I would take a man and 
say, *Here, you take this range and take this man 
for your second, and this for your boiler, and 
these two women, and you run your range and 
let them run theirs.' We had to use negroes in 
that country, of course, with white bosses over 
all. Then I would take another head cook and 
give him his range and his help, and so on for all 
seven ranges. This kitchen took up about all the 
length of the building. There was a store-room 
at one end and the office at the other, and the 
kitchen filled up all the way between ; and there 
was the dining-room adjoining, of course; it was 
four hundred feet long and could seat twelve 
hundred people at once. Well, it had the tables 
divided into seven departments, same as the 
kitchen range, and there were seven head waiters, 
each with his own crew of boys, and they had to 
get their orders filled at their own range and could 
not go to any other, so it was like seven different 
kitchens and seven dining-rooms, but all rimning 
at once, and no confusion. Right there in that 
building they could only acommodate about five 
hundred with rooms, but the grounds were laid 
off like streets; each cottage had four rooms; 
there were ten rows of cottages and forty in each 
row, and a black steward and a set of boys for 
each row, and a white steward to look after them, 
and all those cottage people came to eat in the 
big dining-room. I had two butchers always at 
work, killed thirty-three sheep a day, and chickens 
— well, we gave them all chicken for breakfast 
and supper — twenty-five hundred people at a time 
— and you can judge what it took." 

"But I don't see where you roasted your meat 
with that kind of a range." 

"We had rotary roasters, seven of them." 

"Rotary ovens .^" 

"No, meat roasters ; a sort of cupola with a red- 
hot stove inside; hung the meat on hooks and the 
roaster worked a crank and kept the joints in 
motion round this red-hot fire, and once in a 
while he opened a door and basted the meat with 
a big ladle out of the dripping pan that was in- 
side." 

"Oh, yes, I saw such a roaster in the kitchen of 
the Sherman House, Chicago, a couple of years 
ago, but it was not in use. Are they using the 
same outfit at the Springs now?" 

"No, they're all done away with; why in that 
time when they got down to only a thousand 
gue-ts, they began to talk about 'nobody in the 
house,' and went to cutting down help like they 
mischief, and cut off one range and one section of
