Local Specialties. 

There are certain signs and indications to be 
noted w^hen looking abroad from a hotel window, 
and particularly if the express office be next door, 
which go to show that the hotels do not do all 
the dinner making and party giving that takes 
place in any town or city, and it will not do to be 
too sure that they monopolize all the knowledge 
of affairs of the table and of culinary operations. 
There are packages arriving of wreaths and flow- 
ers from distant city florists, and baskets and sealed 
cans from distant citv caterers and restaurateurs 
with famous names, there are messengers with 
covered trays, there are shipments of china and 
glass and silver and all such things as make proof 
conclusive that every day or two, during the 
party season, somebody is going to have a fine 
table and a fine spread, and a good many other 
somebodies are going to dress grandly and go and 
sit around it, and they are not under obligations 
to any hotels or hotelists either, they seem to 
know how, all by themselves. They know how 
to have thirteen course dinners, but there are no 
indications that they make use of a printed menu. 
It must be quite amateurish dinner giving that is 
going on like amateur theatricals, and probably 
l)oth ladies and gentlemen enjoy it immensely in 
«pite of all the toil and trouble. So it is quite 
reasonable to infer that of all the winter tourists 
now to be seen upon the piazzas of the many 
Florida hotels the great majority have already 
this winter been similarly interested, have all out- 
shone somebody else, and left a pattern for less 
skillful ones to vainly try and come up to before 
they started on their winter tours. 

Now these things ought to be recorded. Money 
is spent lavishly in these social contests and nights 
and days are given up to thinking and toiling, and 
still the fame of anything particularly brilliant, or 
of a new device or a happy thought, or a newly 
acquired secret of a new dish cannot much more 
than reach across the street for the want of a 



society journal devoted to the cultivation of such 
social arts and to reports of every eminent exploit 
in entertainment, such a journal as would give the 
beaten ones at home points and teach them from 
other experts' example, how to beat these tourists 
at their own game when they go back. 

Somebody certainly ought to stai^t a paper 
devoted to party giving and culinary topics. It 
would seem as if New York should be the place 
of publication, but any other large city would 
answer as well. It could not be a hotel paper ex- 
clusively, it would not get support enough, for the 
topics it would treat of would be work to the 
hotel crowd, and they will not pay regular 8ub« 
scriptions for that which is work ; but its subject 
matter would be the play of society people and 
judicious records of their successes, and money 
would be no object The scope of such a journal 
would be as broad as the continent It should 
have the society editors or society reporters of 
every Sunday paper in the land for its correspon- 
dents and would find an exhaustless field as yet 
quite undeveloped in finding out, comparing, classi- 
fying and tracing the local and individual speci- 
alties of the most widely separated communities. 
For it is a singular fact that every town possesses 
same members of society, who boast of a speci- 
alty, generally some special dish which they make 
of peculiar excellence; in many ordinary parties 
the one specialty is the controlling motive for the 
gathering, but when the occasion is an extra- 
ordinary one, such as when a distinguished guest 
has to be entertained and the city hotel is not con- 
sidered to be equal to the emergency, then in ad- 
dition to the town's specialty, every noted indi- 
vidual specialty of the town's own dinner-givers 
is contributed by the several makers, and as a re- 
, suit the banquet table is of course unsurpassable. 

It was only the oth^r day the New York Hotel 
Reporter contained an account of the rise if not 
of the progress of one of these local sp>ecialties in 
the discovery of some man upon the northeastern 
coast that a good stew can be made of mussels as 
well as oysters, and in consequence, everybody 
around was eating mussel stews. Now, as mus- 
sels are very abundant anywhere along the coast, 
the other neighborhoods that will take up the 
specialty of a mussel stew will want to know 
about the individual specialties whether johnny- 
cake or brown bread that are required to complete 
the feast. As meagre as the account is in partic- 
ulars, however, it is evident that but for the news- 
paper mention the many who have been thinking 
that a stew had to be oysters or nothing would 
have known nothing of the mussel ciubs' suc- 
cesses, and when it is considered that every town 
has its special dishes and that while the above in- 
stance occurs in Maine both Jacksonville antf

TTallahassee in Florida boast excellencies of their 
own -which have never been shown up, and which 

Maine is in perfect ignorance about. It is plain 

the new journal of dinner giving and culinary 

4ifiairs would find plenty to do. 



DEVILED OYSTERS IN SHELL. 

These are oysters in a thick sauce baked like 
tleviled crabs in the shells with bread crumbs on 
top. They might be called scolloped oysters just 
as deviled crabs might be called scalloped crabs, 
but they are not. 

Select the deep shells of oysters of good shape; 
they will require careful cleansing with a brush 
and running water. Take the largest oysters of 
the locality, they may by Lynnhaven*s or Chesa- 
peakes, or Norfolk Inlets, or Mobile Plants, or 
Baratarias, or Tampa Bays, or Galveston Bayous, 
they are all right without regard to name if they 
are only large, white, fat and have the natural salt 
taste of sea water. Set them on the scald but not 
boil, then drain them in a colander. Use half 
oyster liquor and half milk, boil it, put in a liberal 
■allowi^nce of good butter, some salt and pepper 
■and thicken with flour mixed in milk. Then 
butter the shells; put in each one four or five of 
the shrunken oysters or what they will hold, fill 
up the shell with a spoonful of the sauce, have 
some finely chopped crumbs of loaf bread ready 
and cover the oysters with them. Bake till light 
brown. While they are baking melt some butter 
■and moisten the top toasted surface with a spoon* 
f ul of it, and when brown enough send the oysters 
in the shells, with lemons cut in fancy shapes for 
garnish and toast to eat with them. 



divided down the back. Throw it into boiling 
water for a few minutes, then into cold ; get out 
the soft marrow by pressing and scraping with a 
knife. Put the marrow into a frying pan with 
butter, pepper and salt, scramble it over the fir«, 
make thin toast in fancy shapes and spread the 
marrow like butter evenly upon it. Set in the 
stove for a minute or two and send it in hot. 



BRAIN PATTIES. 

Parboil a set or two of brains in water, put them 
then in cold water and pick off all the dark skin. 
Take a frying pan and put in a spoonful of butter, 
a teaspoon of minced onion and a tablespoon of 
minced parsley ; put in the brains, two egg9 and 
a spoonful of milk and scramble them over the fire 
like scrambled eggs, while cooking add a slight 
grating of nutmeg and juice of half a lemon^ 
pepper and salt. Bake puff paste palty cases, take 
out the lids and remove surplus paste from the in> 
side; fill with brains, put on the lids and serve 
garnished with parsley. 



MACAROON ICE CREAM. 

Use the soft macaroons which are made same 
way as egg kisses with plenty of crushed almonds 
in to make them brown. Allow a pound of 
macaroons to about three pints of pure cream. 
Sweeten the cream with maraschino, chop maca- 
roons and mix in; freeze as usual, then pack it in 
a brick mould and freeze again solid enough to 
cut in slices. 



BfARROW TOAST — ONE WAY. 

The surpassing merit of these two specialties 
lies solely in the scarcity of marrow and the con- 
sideration that hotels having six hundred people 
can't indulge. 

Take the marrow bone out of a round of beef 
lor every two or three persons and saw it in 
lengths of one inch as far as the marrow extends 
— ^these are the ring bones that may be seen in the 
middle of round of beef steaks. Place close to- 
gether in a pie pan, pepper and salt well, bake in 
•a hot oven to get a brown surface. Make some 
thin toast, cut in shapes, take the marrow out of 
the bones with a teaspoon and place in the middle 
of each piece of toast, the brown side up ; serve hot.

The spinal marrow of an ox is like brains but 
somewhat richer in substance ; the leathery outer 
cord is not eatable. The butchers will save it, 
removing the entire string when the carcass is 



BANANA AMBROSIA. 

Cut bananas in small pieces into a bowl and 
shake some powdered sugar over, cut oranges in 
slices all over the bananas, and cover with grated 
cocoanut and then with powdered sugar again, 
then more bananas and oranges till the bowl is 
full and moisten it with sweet wine. To be served 
as a cold dessert. Add almonds and candy if liked. 



The Florida Shrimp. 



The Florida shrimp being the largest shrimp, 
must be adjudged to carry off the palmetto over 
every shrimp of the Northern zone as the best, 
for there has never been but one serious fault 
found with the shrimp, and that one was its ex- 
asperating smallness. The Florida shrimp shows 
the world of visitors what a shrimp ought to be 
in size. Your Northern shrimp may be well 
enough in flavor and in depth of color, but it re- 
quires the eater to wear magnifying spectacles to 
get any satisfaction out of eating it, hence It is 
chiefly used as a relish with the afternoon tea by 
elderly parties in Boston and the other cities 



I 



THE AMERICAN COOK. 



"where glasses are generally worn. The Florida 
shrimp is different. Then the shrimp ought to 
be skinned or shelled before being eaten, but the 
Northern shrimp is so small, that any healthy 
person, with or without spectacles, could eat them 
faster than any six persons could husk them out, 
consequent y, those who do indulge have to pinch 
them up by the head and tail and bite them off 
close with the shucks on, and all because of the 
liigh price of labor. It is not so with the Florida 
shrimp. The annoying diminutiveness of the 
Northern shrimp has even had an effect upon the 
forms of speech of the people ; the bluff seafarers 
of the coaEt towns do not call a diminutive weak- 
ling there a dude, or any such tame appellation, 
they only call him a little shrimp of a man, and 
that settles it, and him. But they have never seen 
the Florida shrimp, one of the robust, full-grown, 
horny and corpulent old residents. 

So those i».terested in the Northern shrimp in- 
dustry naturally try to make their shrimp appear 
as large as they can. One such man I caught in 
the act of misrepresenting the size of Northern 
shrimp. It was in an old cathedral town, an old, 
ivy-green, ruinous, tumble-down, red-brick, moss- 
covered town, and the old fellow was just as old, 
and had an old basket on his head, and this is 
what he cried as he went along: 

"Fresh shrimps!" 
• "Here's your double D*8." 

"As big as bullock's knees. 

"From the tops of the trees. 

"And the bottoms of the seas. 

"And a many of *em weighs a pound I*' 

I thought there was some wonder in the basket 
and that he had either Florida shrimps or lobsters, 
but it did not turn out so. His shrimps were the 
very small ones, not like the Florida shrimps, of 
which, although so much larger, comparatively 
few would weigh a pound, or more. 

There are shrimp in cans. Southern shrimp of 
good size; they are very good and getting widely 
diffused. There are more brands of canned 
shrimp than one, now ; they are getting better — 
better filled, solid packed, fresher flavored. These 
shrimp are large beyond comparison with the 
Northern shrimp, yet they are but the siftings in 
size, the small fry of the monsters that may be 
seen in the baskets at the Florida markets. It 
would not do to can such as those in the present 
regulation size of can, the buyer would only find 
about four individuals in a can, and these would 
not be the very double D's themselves, for they 
would be shorn of the glory of their wonderful 
horns. Some of the shrimp that come to the 
Florida hotels to be cooked, will measure from the 
point of the beak to the tip of tail eight or nine 
Inches, and to the end of the horns, twelve inches 



more, making a total length of twenty inches ot 
shrimp, and there are several counties yet tc I e 
heard from, for a large part of Florida is as yet 
unsurveyed and scarcely explored, and no one 
who has observed how fast the shrimp increases 
in size as he goes South, will doubt but that un- 
discovered country will some day reveal shrimp- 
at least twice as large as these. Another thing 
that remains undiscovered, I wish some intelligent 
fibhermen would make known, and that is how 
the shrimp manages his horns, so much longer 
than himself, and what he does with them on his 
business trips in the tepid waters of the Gulf 
Stream, or dodging the frolicsome jelly-fish in 
the surf on the sandj' beach. As his remains lie 
subject to inquest in the fish baskets, the Florida 
shrimp*s horns look like unlimited shoe-strings — 
pink mermaid's shoe-strings, twined in tanglefool 
knots about the others, so that in lifting one you 
lift a sti'ing of them. In life does he part thein 
in the middle, let them fall behind and follow 
him at their leisure, or brai J them down the back^ 
or coil them like a Chinaman's que on top of his 
head, or does he use them as oars, or clasp the 
ends of them straight ahead like hands of a bather 
about to dive deep down in the Florida coral 
grove, where tlie purple mullet and gold fish rove, 
or does he simply take his horns straight like 
many another old settler.^ 

Through a series of inferences and deductions, 
I have come to the conclusion that the average 
market price of these large shrimp, is five cents 
per pound, uncooked and on sale like any other 
fish, and they are bought for th^ solid eating that 
is in them by people who are hungry and mean 
business. To take two bites of a cherry and two 
bites of a shrimp is a mark of feeble executive 
ability, but not when the Florida shrimp is ia 
question, for the most energetic eaters having hun 
husked out and lying at length on the plate like a 
good-sized, clean-scraped radish, find it expedient 
to cut him in three portions unless they have an 
abnormal mouth. In order to see these largest 
specimens, however, it is necessary to rise in tlie 
morning and follow the little housekeepers as 
they come out through many a garden gate and 
trip along to the market. Some of them will get 
lost among the banana stands and pine-apple 
stalls, and along sidewalks where the countless 
boxes of oranges and lernons in transit, crowd 
them off into the gutters, but the survivors unde- 
terred press on, and you will find that though on 
pleasure they arc bent, tiiey have a frugal mind 
if vou watch their motions around' the table cov- 
ered with piles of shrimp. Everything is sold by 
the five cents' worth. There are stands with 
stone-tops, zinc-tops, even with marble- tops, and 
upon them are handfuls of shrimp, at five cents

large sheephead or a dozen croakers or a string of 
speckled sea trout They went for shrimp and 
knew they would get them, but the stray fishes 
are special favors of the sea which old Neptun 
throws into their toilful nets for ^''lagniappe^^

The Breakfast Bill of Fare. 



While so much attention is being bestowed at 
this holiday season upon the hotel dinner bills 
of fare, perhaps there ought to be a little con 
sideration of the breakfast bill as well, for if 
the dinner is the more laborious, elaborate and 
expensive meal, the breakfast has the more 
difficult mission of pleasing many of whom do 
not want to be pleased, and will be blamed, 
or will 'blame themselves, if they do be pleased, 
and it comes in place here in this warm and 
flowery land to enquire what can be done 
about it. Before the best breakfast menu ever 
made up and the best breakfast imaginable can 



ever do much good, I think there is something to 
be done in the dining room to make it different 
from what it is now, and although I have looked 
over the portraits of all the competent hotel men 
I ever knew, there was not one that seemed equal 
to this one little task ; there is some other officer 
wanted yet and I can't think who it can be unless, 
perhaps, it is the hotel landlady. Of course it is 
well known that it is altogether against every 
hotel man's principles to have any lady meddling 
in the running of any hotel man's hotel, still if 
they will please not go anywhere else I think they 
might interfere advantageously in the fixing up 
and management of the breakfast room windows 
especially on the green, arboraceous floricultured 
side of the house, but they ought never to need ta 
set foot in the kitchen, because they cannot keep 
from worrying where worry does no good. If 
"this pleasing, anxious beng," the landlord's wife, 
could only take a cigar and some philosophy and 
contentment, and sit outside on the porch, the same 
as her husband does, and let the machine run itself 
after the necessary oil has been supplied to run it 
with, then it would be different. However, I have 
seen inside some of the private houses and while 
I would not give much for the cooking they do, 
nor the tables they set, it must be owned their 
windows look better than ours, for when a hotel 
man makes a dining room just as fine as he knows 
how, for the last touch of furnishing he boards up 
all his windows with hideous slat-bar blinds and 
thinks that is happiness. It seems to me it would 
be jollier to eat breakfast outside, under a Florida 
fig tree, with the vari-colored Florida chameleons 
picking up the crumbs that fiill from the rich 
men's table, than in such a solemn shop as that 
makes. What is wanted is for the lady of the 
house to wait till the hotel man goes to NVw York 
to engage his help for the season, then order all 
those blinds taken down, and then fix up the win- 
dows her own way. That way is not easy to 
describe but, as near as it can be told, they get for 
the tops of each window a harlequin or lambre- 
quin, or some such word ; they hang on that some 
white mosquito-bar stuff that drapes both sides of 
the window like Spanish moss hanging from the 
cypress and myrtle ; they gather up the slack and 
hang it over a great golden hook on each side, and 
then there is something else, it is a crimson tassel 
hanging down fuschia-flower fashion in the middle 
of the window. If this attempted description has 
only served to plunge the subject into still deeper 
mystery, I might get drawings made and have the 
whole thing set to music so that the parties to be 
interviewed may be i-^duced to read it But per- 
haps all the necessary articles are to be found in 
furnishing stores. When that is done raise the 
window a little, and Ux in the alabaster light from

HOURS FOR MEALS. 
BreakList, 6:30 to 10. Dinner, i to 3. Supper, 6 to 8:3a 

An extra charge will be made for dishes ordered not on the 
Bill of Fare, and for Meals, Fruits, etc., served in rooms. 

Somebody has remarked concerning disputed 
points in the arrangement of bills of fare that 
there is no ultimate authority to settle such 
questions, forgetting what a government we have 
and how every great and difficult question of 
public polity is referred back to th^ primary source 
of power, the people themselves for settlement. 
The forms of bills of fare vary, but the people who 
come and go order their meals always in nearly 
the same rotation. The above arrangements fits 
them, as the saying is, like the paper on the wall. 
First is the heading "Fruit,** and under it a blank 
line long enough to write in the particular kind. 
It would not do to print in the names of the fruits 
in Florida, there are too many changes, too many 
fruits, they run from a to 2, from apricots to Zan- 
zibar plantains, besides the etc., such as Japanese 
plums and mango apples, and there may be reason 
for putting even these last on the table and on the 
bill of fare sometimes. For where there is a man 
that has not at some time heard a ^'dark-eyed maid 
■with her sweet guitar" sing 

**0, I want to gt> 
To the land where the mango apples grow." 

Well, that's Florida. Mangoes grow down 
south below the frost line, and, wherever they 
come from there are plenty of them in the markets. 
They look very well, something like a pawpaw. 



and look as though they ought to be good to eat 
It is said tliat every seven years the human body 
is renewed and the tastes change, and some who 
dislike celery or other peculiar flavors in youth, 
change to a great liking for them later in life. 
Every seven years, therefore, every person tastes 
a mango to see whether their time to like it has 
come around yet This is why we have a blank 
space left to write it in . 

It is equally necessary to have a blank line 
under the next heading, "Fish," for as there are 
generally too many items in the breakfast bill, 
even oysters in all styles become commonplace if 
printed there and are a more attractive dish if 
written in afresh. If you do in the South as South- 
ernes do, there will appear in that lime^'tenderloin 
of trout," a thing discovered and patented here. 
We have no State rights for sale; people must 
come to Florida for it Then, sometimes, besides 
the ordinary run of breakfast fishes there will be 
"snapper au court-bouillon," or, "a la Palatka,'* 
and, again, that first-class delicacy, mullet roes—* 
both soft and hard roes— breaded and fried. 

- In the remaining blank under the meats comes 
the chicken, fried, stewed, or a la marengo, or the 
quail or venison steak, or any special message 
there may be. It makes but little difference what 
it may be, the written line is always read first, it 
may be all that is read ; it is taken as a special 
notice, there is a sort of write-me-a-letter-from- 
home - and-tell-me-youVe-got-pig's-feet-for-break- 
fast feeling in regard to it, and then it is a sign 
that it is a fresh edition, like the morning paper, 
and not the the same old breakfast we always have. 

DINNERS IN COURSES. 



(Prom Articles Written for the Jacksonville, Fla., Ulu* 
strated Hotel News.) 



They were very high society people who first 
caused me to take notice with what perfect ease 
such people can order a dinner in courses from 
the regular hotel bill of fare when they are so 
minded. There were eight or nine in the party; 
they occupied one table. The head of the party 
was the wealthy owner of one of the greatest 
newspapers in this country, but I do not like to 
make free with names, as the only object is to 
show that the straight and sensible bill of fare 
used in the best hotels is all sufficient for any 
style. 

This was at a delightful resort, in a hotel, not 
too fine, but one I had heard of when a thousand 
miles away, and months before I saw it; heard of 
it not for the French that was or was not put in 
the bill of fare, but because its tables were remark* 
able for being always decorated with hot- house 
flowers. 






THE AMERICAN COOK. 



aut 



When this party came to dinner the gentleman 
at the head of the table took the bill of fare him- 
self, and instead of submitting it to each one to 
order what they might please, he ordered one 
course for all, selecting the meat, the sauce, and 
one or two vegetables, and a very small portion 
for each. When that course was ended the plates 
and silver were required to be exchanged and an- 
other course was ordered from the same bill, and 
so it proceeded throug hnine or ten or even twelve 
-courses, a change of silver and plates and glasses 
being required for each course and one waiter with 
^a little outside assistance kept in constant attend- 
ance for the two hours' duration of each dinner. 
They found everything needful to make a choice 
-and varied dinner ' in that straight bill of fare. 
After the first day the steward caused to be 
prepared a light soda punch or strawberry Ro- 
maine and although it was placed at the bottom 
of the bill beside the Regular ice cream, this party 
•observed and knew when to order it, as a course 
between the entree and the roast fowl. They 
femained about three weeks, always adhering to 
the same habit. When a hotel advertises rates 
irom three to five dollars per day, the five dollar 
rate is for such parties as this who require more 
service and better than the majority. The table 
d'hdte dinner was so much more than sufiicient 
that had a party at the next table and the next 
chosen to dine in the same way, Ihey could each 
have selected courses from the same bill different 
from either of the other tables. 



At the time when a public banquet awaited 
■Oeneral Grant, whichever way he might happen 
to turn, one was prepared in his honor in a west- 
ern city of great celebrity in its day. The Mayor 
and city council -were the movers and other lead- 
ing citizens joined. Two hundred and fifty invi- 
tations were issued. The banquet waa given in 
the principal hotel. TheV-e was plenty of available 
wealth to make the affair a brilliant one, but the 
isolation of the locality, made the procurement of 
luxuries as difiicult as it was costly. A sumptuous 
banquet was secured, however, and in course of 
preparation. The steward in charge could not 
•entertain the idea of using a bill of fare of the 
ordinary routine pattern, but decided that the 
dinner must be served in courses, and that there 
must be a frozzen punch in the proper place, and 
the menu must be printed in corresponding form ; 
which, accordingly was done, and, which was 
unfortunate under the circumstances, as one of 
the printed menus was placed at every plate. 
A large proportion of the guests were boarders at 
this and other hotels and were accustomed to 
•order dinner from the straight American plan bill 
<»f fare. 



The soup being ready in place it was passed 
along in due course when the guests sat down to 
table, but instead of any other course following, 
about the first word was from a waiter: 

**A man says he doesn't want any fish but he'll 
take quail and asparagus." 

*'But he can't have it," answered the steward 
excitedly.** Did'nt I tell you this is a course din- 
ner? The asparagus goes with the sweet-breads, 
and the quail doesn't come on till after the punch.'* 

**Oh — I did start to say something, but the man 
says he knows what's good, he sees it in the bill 
of fare and he wants it, and I'll never go near him 
any more.'* So that waiter slid off, but there 
were fifty waiters, and if the steward had been 
listened to, in ten minutes there would have been 
a riotous confusion, and the banquet would have 
been a failure and a disgrace; instead of that 
occuring, the cooks and carvers immediately 
dropped into their every-day habit, served to each 
waiter his order as nearly as possible and as quietly, 
without the smallest heed to the planned courses^ 
and all went smooth and tlie guests got what they 
wanted. 

Then there came the usual few minutes of quiet 
and the steward rallied himself to get the punch 
served in the proper place. He did not want to 
give up as one totally defeated, and, besides, the 
punch was of the finest quality, such as costs 
about twenty-five cents a glass, like Imperial or 
Victoria punch, made of champagne, cordials, and 
the juice of imported fruits; there was enough 
provided for two hundred and fifty ; it had cost 
perhaps fifty dollars to prepare. The steward had 
it filled into punch glasses, carried in, and set one 
to each plate. But it was not received with a 
welcome. Doubtless some among the many knew 
what it was, and what for, as every guest was a 
somebody, else he or she would not have been 
there, but while some let the glass remain indif- 
ferently, a good number were offended and made 
the waiters carry it out again, scowling and saying 
they were not ready for their ice-cream yet, as if 
they thought the waiters were trying to curtail 
the length of their dinner; and when near the last, 
the real ice-cream was handed around, those who 
had accepted the punch 'pushed this aside, saying 
they had already had their ice-cream. There are 
several life size morals in this relation, but I have 
not time to draw them. 



The way to make a fine and remarkable bill o£ 
fare is to have ripe strawberries on New Year's 
Day, when they cost a dollar apiece, or the first 
shad of the season, or some young elephant meat 
from Africa, or anything that the other house 
can't get

SCBAPS ABOUT EDIBLES. 



A Typioal American Dish. 

The American edible clam of the Atlantic seaboard 
is not much larger than our scallop or scollop. 
Baw, it does duty for the oyster * au naturel;*' and 
in this simple condition it was likewise devoured 
by the Romans, vinegar being sometimes replaced 
by oxymel. When clams were eaten cooked, the 
disciples of Apicius and Lucullus placed the mol- 
luscs in a new stewpan with a little oil, sweet wine, 
and pepper. The coction was completed over a 
slow fire, and before serving much more pepper was 
added to the stew. Mrs. Hales — the Miss Acton 
of the United States — gives minute directions for 
frying, stewing, and steaming both hard and soft 
shell-clams, and for making clam-fritters; but,oddIy 
enough, she omits any mention of clam-chowder. 
The observant M. Urbain Dubois, however, in his 
■'Cosmopolitan Cookery/' gives a sufficiently lucid 
*'apercu*' of clam-chowder; only he treats it as a 
*<pota'ge." **Clam-chowder« soup," the German 
Kaiser's cA^lell us, is made from the chopped flesh 
of clams placed in a well-buttered stewpan, and 
••accommodated" with onions blanched and minced, 
and a bunch of aromatics, salt, pepper, nutmeg, 
cayenne, and mace, the whole moistened with a 
■ufficienoy of wine and Csh-broth. Prior to serving 
the soup is to be thickened with a handful of bruised 
"crackers," and fortified — for a mess of five dozen 
olams with a bottle of Rhine wine. This is nearly, 
but not quite, the genuine article. M. Dubois has 
omitted an integral component of chowder, the pork. 
To find the **norma," or original basis, of chowder, 
we must go back to the venerable Mrs. Hannah 
Qiaase, in whose culinary '*Novum Organum," and 
under the heading of "A Cheshire Pork-Pyefor 
Sea," to which she specially directs the attention 
of master-mariners, there will be found the real 
foundation of chowder. **Take," says Hannah, 
fome salt pork which has been boiled; cut it into 
thin s.ices; an equal quantity of potatoes, pared, 
and cut thin; lay a layer of pork seasoned with 
pepper, and a layer of potatoes; then another layer 
of pork, and so on till your pye is full. Then add 
more pepper, lay some butter on the top, and fill 
your dish about half-full of soft water. Cover up 
close, and bake it in a gentle oven. 

This is veritable chowder, and in the British navy 
was, during many generations, extensively patron- 
ized by on reliant tars, by whom it was known as 
*'sea-pie," and sometimes as **lob0cou8e." On the 
shores of New England, however, it was popular- 
ized as ''chowder," and with the addition of the 
•and-clams, which were, so amazingly popular, it 
beeame "olam-ohowder."

A publication announces that there are daily eateo 
in London some thousands of the hind-quarters of 
frogs, "and truly delicious they are when nicely 
cooked in butter till of a rich brown color." We 
have never "spotted" the dish on a restaurant menu, 
and believe the statement to be incapable of verifi- 
cation. Apropos cf frog-eating, however, we note 
that the Societe Protectrice des Anrmaux has issued 
a strong protest against the present mode of pro- 
viding frogs for the dinner-table in France. It ap* 
pears that the poor creatures when caught have the 
upper part of their legs, or edible portion of their 
bodies, ruthlessly cut off with a pair of shears. The 
frogs in their mutilated state being helpless, they 
are thrown aside. Numbers of them are stated to 
have been found eight or ten days after their mutil- 
ation crawling about on their fore-legs in a pitable 
condition. The society, therefore, recommends thai 
some plan of killing them in the first place should 
be adopted. 



How to Ck>ok a Oanvasbaok Duok. 



To roast a canvasback duck, pluck the duck ex- 
cept the wings and head. Cut off the wings. Draw 
the whole inside and windpipe. Put alcohol in » 
small flat pan; set fire to it, and hold the duck over 
it one minute. Clean the duck by rubbing with a. 
dry cloth. Cut off the neck and head. Take the 
skin off the head and remove the eyes. Put tht» 
head inside the duck, with the end of the bill just 
sticking out; season inside with salt and pepper,, 
and truss in the ordinary fashion. The web feet 
are not cut off. Roast on the spit for about fif- 
teen minutes more or less, according to size. 

To broil a canvas-back duok. Clean as for roast- 
ing. Split the duck on the back, season and anoint 
with sweet oil. Put the duck in a double gridiron 
with hinge. Cook over a very brisk fire for about 
twelve minutes. When placed on a dish pour over 
it melted butter, with lemon juice, salt, pepper and 
chopped parsley. Canvasbaoks should be served 
on hot plates.

Turtle a la Ohinolse. 

The flesh of turtles forms almost the staple food 
of tbe natives of large districts in tbe tropics, and 
is cooked in seyeral ways. No method of culinary 
preparation that we ever beard of, however, would 
be more likely to please both gourmet and gourmand 
than tbe one credited to tbe fastidious citizens of 
Pekin. If you follow it, you will take a live turtle 
that you have previously deprived of anything to 
drink long enough to render him exceedingly 
thirsty; you will place him in a caldron of cool 
water in such a position that his body will be im- 
mersed, but that be will be unable to get bis mouth 
down to it: at tbe side of the kettle, within reach of 
jBis turtlesbip, you will then place a bowl of cool 
and spicy wine. This done, set the caldron on the 
lire, and observe with glee tbe enrichment of your 
noble repast. Urged by thirst, the turtle eagerly 
drinks the wine; and as the slowly beating water in 
which he floats grows hotter and hotter, bis thirst 
Increases, and he drinks deeper and deeper of tbe 
wine, until suddenly be is boiled, and dies, full of 
wine, and fragrant tbrough the uttermost fibers of 
his unctuous flesh with the rich condiment he has 
80 plentifully imbibed. Luxury and art have reach- 
ed their acme ! — Harper* t Bazar, 



In the course of a conversation with a dealer in 
game, a Philadelphia Press reporter learned that 
within a few weeks past, since the advent of cold 
weather, a few venturesome spirits residing in that 
city decided to thoroughly test the value of tbe meal 
of tbe rat as an article of diet. The rodents bad 
been caught and caged wbile young, and fed care- 
fully upon grain and green food. The rats thrived 
upon tbe diet and their silky coats gave evidence 
of a thoroughly healthful condition. At tbe meal 
in question they had been carefully prepared, and 
were served with other viands. The flesh, after 
cooking, was found to be quite light in color, much 
more so than either tbe rabbit or the squirrel, and 
possessing a delicacy of flavor entirely unknown to 
either of the last mentioned animals. The experi- 
ment proved entirely successful and a diet of rats 
prepared under proper conditions was voted to be 
practical and economical.

Hsooked. — Ex,

What Tripe Is. 



[Barlington Hawkeye.] 



Oceasionaly you see a man order tripe at a hotel, 
but he always looks hard, as though he hated him- 
self and everybody else. He tries to look as though 
he enjoyed it, but he does not. Tripe is indiges- 
tible, and looks like an India rubber apron for a 
child to sit on. When it is pickled it looks like 
dirty clothes put to soak, and when it is cooking it 
^ooks as though the cook was- boiling a dish cloth. 
On the table it looks like glue and tastes like a piece 
of old silk umbrella cover. A stomach that is not 
lined with corrugated iron would be turned wrong 
side out by the smell of tripe. A man eating tripe 
at a hotel tat>le looHs like an Arctic explorer dining 
on his boots or chewing pieces of frozen dog. You 
cannot look at a man eating tripe but he will blush 
and look as though he wanted to apologize and con- 
vince you he is taking it to tone up his system. A 
woman never eats tripe. There is not money enough 
in the world to hire a woman to take a corner of & 
sheet of tripe in her teeth and try to pull off a piece. 
Those who eat tripe are men who have had their 
stomachs play mean tricks on them, and they eat 
tripe to get even with their stomach8,and then they 
go and take a Turkish bath to sweat it out of their 
system. Tripe is a superstition handed down from 
a former generation of butchers, who sold all the 
meat and kept the tripe for themselves and the dogs, 
but the dogs of the present day will not eat tripe. 
You throw a piece of tripe down in front of a dog 
and see if he does not put his tail between his legs 
and go ofif and hate you. Tripe may have a value, 
but it is not as food. It may be good to fill in a 
burglar-proof safe, with the cement and chilled 
steel, or it might answer to use as a breast plate in 
the time of war, or it would be good to use for 
bumpers between cars, or it would make a i^ood 
face for the weight of a pile driver, but when you 
come to smuggle it into the stomach you do wrong. 
Tripe! Bah! A piece of Turkish towel soaked in 
axle grease would be pie compared with tripe. 



Redsnapper loses its fine flavor by being sent 
North on ice, and is best eaten where it is caught, 
say epicures. 



Roast grouse are often ruined by being allowed 
to stand after being taken from the fire, and thug 
become dry and parched. All game tastes best 
that is sent immediately ftrom the (ire to the dinner 
table.

the king. 



Moahrooms Not Poiaonous. 

People must talk and write paragraphs, but one 
that is now going the rounds should not be allowed 
to raise a new prejudice against the delicious and 
and wholesome edible mushroom, that is but just 
beginning to be appreciated in this country. The 
statement is made and backed up with some foreign 
name of a doctor that mushrooms are poisonous 
always; that the water they are boiled in is more 
poisonoub; that mushrooms in the raw state are 
most poisonous, and their poisonous properties do 
not depart from them until they are dried and kept 
a certain number of days. It does not say whether 
they are to be dried in the dark of the moon or not. 
But the statement goes on that a dog fed on mush- 
rooms died in a certain number of days from their 
eflfects. We do not know about the dog, there being 
no witnesses named, but think we could have killed 
him with peaches and cream, or peach and honey, 
or rock and rye, or almost any other thing besides 
mushrooms, if we had had a motive for it. It is 
not long since we read that a dog died because it 
was fed so many days on bread. Still, we have not 
given up eating bread. It has also been stated that 
the bread we eat is more or less poisonous, so is 
the meat, so is the air, the water, the paper on the 
walls, the paste it is put on with, the soap we use, 
the coffee, tea, flavoring extracts, tobacco, but they 
seem to be amazingly slow in operation. Some 
poisons are really quite wholesome and pleasant. 
Arsenic, as is well known, is quite extensively 
eaten for its fattening properties, and mushrooms 
cannot certainly be any worse than arsenic. That 
they are not, is shown in their consumption in large 
quantities daily in the hotels and restaurants; and 
the sauces are made with the liquor they are boiled 
in. The customers make meals of them, the cooks 
make meals of them — these are the canned. Fresh 
mushrooms are cooked and eaten all through the 
season when they are obtainable, and some kinds 
that are not true mushrooms are sold and bought 
and cooked and served with equal harmlessness. 
The writer has gathered mushrooms and eaten them 
raw, as children do, in the fields where they grew 
in abundance, and stole the ketchup before it was 
finished making and absorl>ed mushroom poison in 
overy way, along with bread poison, coffee poison, 
and all the rest. There is no need of proving that 
all these things are not poisonous as long as they 
continue to prove wholesome and beneficial, and no 
need to prove that even those foreign named doc- 
tors are quite harmless as- long as matters turn out 
so lerions with the unhappy canines. 

In England 200 years ago pies and pastries were 
nade of all sorts of good thingb — artichokes, mar- 



row, dates, raisins, figs and ginger — and it is re*^ 

lated that Page invited Falstaff and his friends to a. 
dinner of **hot venison pastry," wound up by **pip^ 

pins and cheese.'* 



The Various Fryingr Mixtures, from 
Olive Oil to Butter and Lard. 

From The Caterer. 



There are several oily and fatty substances used 
'or frying, which we name in the order of their 
cost. Olive oil is almost exclusively used in olive- 
bearing countries as the cheapest frying material. 
Here it is quite costly and but little used, save by 
the wealthy or the epicure who prizes it for the olive 
flavor it communicates to food cooked in it. Others, 
again, dLslike and reject it for the same reason. 
Clarified butter comes next in cost and is prepared 
as follows: Put the butter into an enameled sauce- 
pan and melt it gently over a clear fire; when it 
begins to simmer take it off the fire, skim well, let 
stand in a warm corner till the buttermilk or cheesy 
matter has settled, then pour it off steadily from, 
the sediment, through fine muslin, into a stone or 
glass jar, cover and keep in a cool place. It is the 
best of all frying material and greatly superior to 
lard, in that the slight flavor it communicates ia 
quite pleasant and appetiiing. 

A third preparation, a favorite with many of tb» 
best European cooks, and a genuine mixture,is com^ 
posed of equal measure of olive oil, butter, veal suet 
and leaf lard. The butter is first melted and stirred 
into the other three, already mixed and melted; 
then it is strained into a stone pot and kept always 
in cold place, well covered. The combined flavor 
of the four ingredients is acceptable to almost all 
tastes. 

Fresh butter comes next and is much to be pre- 
ferred to lard, but it has one objectionable quality. 
On account of the buttermilk and salt it contains it 
scorches and burns when subjected to a high or 
long-continued heat. This renders it unfit for the 
cooking of many delicate dishes. This tendency 
can, however, be much lessened by rubbing th» 
frying-pan with a small muslin bag filled with pre* 
pared bee suet. 

Lard is the common, well-nigh universal frying 
material in America, because it is cheap and to b» 
had in every nook and corner of the land. Its frc» 
use has caused many a dyspeptic stomach. If used 
at all only the best leaf lard should be employed 
and xendered out by steam or boiling water, so aa 
to avoid the burnt taste it gets if rendered on tha 
open fire. 



There are times, no doubt, when a oiviliied man 
may eat liver and enjoy it; but these times occur 
but seldom, and to most persons never. To tb» 
shipwrecked mariner, tossed in his frail boat upoa 



the pitiless sea, the stock of old boots exhausted 
and all his companions eaten, then a small piece of 
ofliTcris not altogether unacceptable. (Said by 
one who doesn't know.)

Salads and Salad-Making. 

From London Society. 

The obvious accompaniment to cold meats is salad, 
which may be truly said to fill the bowl which cheers 
but not inebriates. No wonder that, tradition 
tells us it takes three people to make a good salad; 
a sage, to contribute the salt; a miser, to add the 
vinegar, and a prodigal, to pour in the oil. To 
which may be added an untiring steam avm or elec 
trie motor, to stir up the mixture for an indefinite 
time. For, if *<when taken, to be well shaken" is 
applicable to anything that enters the human 
stomach, it assuredly is to the assemblage of ingre- 
dients which go to make a finished salad. In de- 
fault of an automatic mechanical salad-mixer, it is 
the host's duty to perform that task; and it is polite on 
hie part to help himself first, because the beet lies at 
the bottom of the bowel, /b^ufr.to fatigue the salad, 
is the French expressive description of how it ought 
to be turned over and over; so much so, that *'Je 
vais te fairs la salade" is a popular threat that a 
good drubbing instead of a good time, is coming. 
Another saying, "Bataillons de salade," battalions 
drawn promisciously from divers and sundry corps 
of soldiers, is founded on the multiplicity of herbs 
eligible for the composition of a salad. The hemp 
plant was known as **SaladedeGascogne," Qascony 
salad, because it furnished ropes wherewith male- 
factors in the South of France were hanged. By 
such salad many a one has been choked, who pre- 
viously had cuUitivaled the cause of his death — 
theereby suflfering a much worse malady than that 
implied by the proverb.

Est en danger d'est^e malade.''

The Evolution of Bread. 



Seagulls* esgs wsre served at a recent dinner in 
Halifax, given to some Government officials, and 



Persons of extreme views are apt to maintain 
that all mankind, being normally savages, were aa 
normally cannibals; but, leaving that moot question 
altogether on one side, it seems probable that 
humanity ate acorns long before they ate cereals or 
learned the art of making bread, and that the ven- 
eration entertained by the Druids of Gaul and 
Britain for the oak was due to the circumstance 
that its glands were the staple food of the people. 
Bread, properly so called, was transmitted by the 
Greeks to the Romans; and either the latter or the 
Phoenicians may have introduced the cultivation of 
corn into Gaul. While, however, the land was 
mainly covered with immense forests, a long time 
must have elapsed before the practice of eating 
acorns, chestnuts, and beech most was abandoned, 
and even when com was regularly grown, ripened 
and harvested the grains were merely plucked 
from the ear and eaten raw or slightly parched. 
The next step was to infuse the grain in hot water 
for the making of a species of gruel or porridge,, 
and a long time afterwards it may have occurred t& 
some bright genius to pound the corn in a mortar 
or rub it to a powder between two stones. Subse- 
quently came the handmill; but it was not until 
after the First Crusade that the windmill was in- 
troduced from the East, whither it had probably^ 
found its way from China The first bread was 
evidently baked on the ashes and unleavened, and 
the intolerable pangs of indigestion brought on by 
a continual course of *'galette" or *'damper" may 
have suggested the uso of a fermenting agent, whicli 
in the first instance was probably stale bread turned 
sour. Pliny has distinctly told us in bis "Natural 
History" that the Gauls leavened their bread with 
yeast made from the lye of beer; yet, strangely 
enough, they abandoned the une of beer yeast, and 
did not resume it until the middle of the seventeenth 
century. Its revival in France made the fortune 
of many ba hers; then the medical faculty sounded 
an alarm, declaring th t yeast made from beer was 
poisonous. lis employment was prohibited by law 
in 1666, but the outcry raised by the bakers and 
the public was so vehement that in the following 
year the decree of prohibition was oancealed, with 
proviso that the yeast was to be procured only 
from beer freshly brewed in Paris or the immediate 
neighborhood. Some form of fermented bread, 
however, the French had been eating for 1,600 



years in contradistiDCtion to the gruel and pulse- 
vating Italians and Levantines and the purely Tege- 
<carian Hindus. 



Amerioan Pie. 



The foreign visitor to these shores has, with very 
few exceptions, denounced pie as a deadly inven- 
tion of some culinary Satan. He has gazed with 
mingled pity and horror upon the native pie-eater, 
and has often been tempted to stretch out a hand 
to save him from a life of suffering and dyspepsia. 
-Coming from a nation where pie is treated with no 
less contempt than is bestowed by Herr Bismarck 
upon the inoffensive and salutary American hog, he 
is unable to understand by what unlucky chance 
the American people have become a nation of pie- 
eaters. Every disagreeable peculiarity of American 
society he attributes to pie. Pie is responsible f jr 
every variety of evil in our politics. Ruffianism 
and crime are due to pie, and pie, indeed, is the 
•source of almost as many ills as **that forbidden 
fruit whose mortal taste brought death into the 
world, and all our woe." Yet in spite of foreign 
scorn and prejudice the pie habit survives and each 
^ear adds thousands and thousands to the adorei:s 
of pie. The American love for pie can never be 
conquered. It is the strongest proof of American 
birth. The person who does not eat pie is regarded 
by Americans with distrust, and foreigners who do 
«at it are hailed as brothers. The United States 
\>ill experience a thrill of satisfaction and good 
fi-eling to hear that the Czar has ordered 1,000,000 
pics for his coronation ceremonies. It will rejoice 
to learn that there is at least one foreign nation 
that does not share the hostility felt by other great 
powers toward pie. Russia and America have 
always been on friendly terms, but this gratifying 
proof that the Czar is alive to the beauty and excel- 
lence of pie will unite them in the strongest bonds 
of sympathy and good-will. 



Somethinff About Salad Oil. 



N. Y. Sun. 



The gourmand who carefully makes up his own 
dish of cool looking salad is very apt to be deceived 
into believing that the rich gold colored oil he pours 
upon it is from the land of olives. It is an almost 
even chance that it is from the land of cotton, for 
thr sale of cotton seed oil for olive oil has become 
80 expensive that the Italian Government has begun 
to tak<9 strong me«tsures toward keeping the former 
product out of Italy, where it is taken in Italian 
Tesse>i from New Orleans, to be bottled and labeled, 
and /etMrned to this country, so that merchants can 
say that it is imported. But, to those who dread 
the substitution of cotton seed oil for olive oil, there 



is comfort in the fact that the supply of the native 
product is limited, for planters whose lands are thin 
prefer to return the seed to them, and the cotton 
lands of the lower Mississippi, which do not need 
careful fertilizing, furnish the seed for the seventy 
cotton seed oil mills in the South. This enterprise 
is bound to remain confined to the South, for the 
seed is so bulky that transportation would not be 
profitable. That the manufacture of cotton seed 
oil, however, will increase is beyond doubt, as the 
raw seed goes through processes that nearly treble 
its value, and its' oil is being used for paint and also 
for lubricating machinery. 



An Inoredible Story. 



Pall Mall Gazette. 

Not only has the intellect of the worm been sadly 
unappreciated for centuries till Mr. Darwin rehab- 
ilitated that sagacious reptile, but it appears now 
that his value as a viand has also been grossly mis- 
understood and underrated. A group of French 
gourmets, whose object it is to do for the cookery 
of the future what Wagner is doing for its music, 
are happily following up tha labors of Darwin in 
this direction, and, having recently tried this 
tempting morsel, have communicated to a grateful 
public the result of their researches. Fifty guests 
were present at the experiment. The worms, ap- 
parently lob-worms, were first put into vinegar, by 
which process they were made to disgorge the fam- 
ous vegetable mold about which we have heard so 
much. They were then rolled in batter and put 
into an oven, where they acquired a delightful goMen 
tint, and, we are assured, a most appetizing smell. 
After the first p'ateful the fifty guests rose like one 
man and asked for more. Could anything be more 
convincing? Those who love snails, they add, will 
abandon them forever in favor of worms. And yet 
M. Monselet, the great authority in Paris, has told 
us sadly that no advances have been made in the 
art of cooVery since Brillat-Savarin, and that all 
enthusiasm on the subject died out with Vatel when 
he committed suicide because the fish had not ar- 
rived for the royal dinner. 



It was the Duke of Wellington, we believe, who 
referred to hash as "something left over ftrom the 
fight of yesterday," but at some hotels they make 
it so nicely of lamb and potatoes that even epicures 
have expressed satisfaction with it. 



There are three dishes, it is said, which if put 
upon the bill of fare of a London club, are devoured 
before all the rest; so that at 7 or 8 o'clock, when 
most members dine, there Is nothing left of them. 
These dishes are Irish stew, tripe and onions, and 
liver and bacon. 



SCRAPS ABOUT GREAT BATBRS 
AND BPIOURBS. 



Queer Oustozners of Oafes. 

In M. Eugene Chavette's witty and curious little 
Tolume, published in 1867, '* Restaurateurs et Res- 
taures," some eDtertaining portraits are gi?en of 
eccentric guests, celebrated at Parisian cafes. One 
of the most famous of these was Gourier, commonly 
called *'The Fork of Death," a frequenter of the 
Restaurant Bonvelet, who iovited a victim to dine 
with him by the year, and slew him with high feed- 
ing. The first died of apoplexy after a six months' 
combat; the second held his own for two years, and 
then succumbed to a "liver complaint'* — an indi- 
gestion brought on by over-indulgence in the liver 
of the Strasburg goose, * 'three days after," as 
Qourier sadly said, when gazing on the funeral from 
the window of the restaurant, '*I bad treated him 
to a new hat for his birthday." A third champion 
then descended into the arena, a long lean man 
named Ameline, who said as his invariable grace 
when sitting down to table with his host, "You ras- 
cal, Fra going to bury you;" while the host gently 
replied by way of *'Amen,'* "Nonsense; the other 
two said the same thing." The crafty Ameline. 
however, took occasion to pick a quarrel monthly 
with his amphitryon, and, retiring sulkily to his 
tent, dieted himself on tea, toast, and senna, re- 
turning to the encounter mollified and refreshed 
after an absence of two or three days, during which 
Courier lost still more ground by eating rapidly> 
and injuring his digestion by solitary and gloomy 
reflections. One day, after this duel had lasted 
three years, Gourier, who had just helped himself 
to a fourteenth slice of 4-year-old Welsh mutton, 
threw his head back. His companion, thinking he 
was about to sneeze, muttered the customery bene- 
diction; but Gourier fell forward into the currant 
jelly, dead as the mutton he so dearly loved. He 
who had taken the fork had perished by the fork. 
He should have imitated the prudent diner of the 
Cafe Riche,who always had two dozen saucers piled 
at his left when he sat down to table, and wore one 
between his collar and the nape cf his neck through- 
out the repast, changing it as it became warm, as a 
preventive against apoplexy. 



. The heroes of gastronomy, including Mr. Walton 
himself, will gnash their teeth, which, excepting 
their stomachs, must be regarded as thetr most 
Taluable possessions, when they hear of the exploit 
just accomplished by Thomas Cute, of Mount 
Morris, N.T. On Feb. 6 that individual ate six 
quarts of sauer kraut within the space of thirty- 
seven minutes, and washed it down with a bottle of 
champagne. Having survived this feat in excel- 



lent condition he now offers to bet a reasonabl^^ 
amount that he can eat eight quarts of sauer kraut 
within an hour. This challenge is likely to result 
in an international contest, for Clute is not a Ger- 
man, and the children of the Fatherland will not 
tamely submit to his imputation on their capacity 
in the sauer kraut line.

What Bismarck Bats. 

There seems to be something in the air and life 
«f Germany extraordinarily favorable to the diges- 
tion. Bismarck has thriven on mixtures of cham- 
pagne and porter washing down meals at the de- 
scription of which the American trembles, but he 
does no more than the other most famous ruler of 
his country, Frederick the Great. Here is what 
Dr. Zimmerman saw him devour when a septuagen- 
■arian invalid: "A very large quantity of soup, of 
the strongest and most highly spiced ingredients. 
Yet spiced as it already was, he added to each plate 
of it a large spoonful of powdered ginger and mace; 
then a good piece of bceuf a la Russe — beef steeped 
in half a pint of brandy. Next he took a great 
^quantity of an Italian dish, half Indian corn, half 
Parmesan cheese. To this the juice of garlic is 
added, and the whole is baked in butter until there 
•arises a hard rind as thick as a finger. This, one 
of the King's most darling dishes, is called Polenta. 
At last, the King having expressed his satisfaction 
at the excellent appetite which the dandeloin gave 
him, closed the scene with a whole plateful of eel 
pie, so hot and fiery it seemed as if it had been 
baked in hell. At other times he would eat a large 
-quantity of chilling and unwholesome fruits, es- 
pecially melons, and then again a vast number of 
sweetmeats. 



Byron's extravagant fondness for macaroni has 
been recorded in more than one sketch of his tastes 
and habits, but his biographers have omitted to 
mention the fact that he was wont to bestow his 
macaroni so thickly with slices of truffle that the 
result — his favorite dish — might have been more 
correctly described as "truffles au macaroni" than 
as ''macaroni aux truffles." 



There are many examples on record of a voracity 
almost incredible, and sometimes, indeed, including 
the most unlikely objects. Sparing my readers any 
such details, I prefer to relate two actual instances 
from my own experience, which do not require on 
4heir part any great effort of faith. 

Some forty years ago I went to pay a flying visit 



to the vicar of Bregnier, a man of great stature, and 
known throughout the district for his power of 
eating. Though scarcely midday, I found him 
already at the table; the soup had been removed, as 
well as the meat boiled in it, and these two regular 
dishes had been followed by a leg of mutton, a fine 
fowl and a large bowl of salad. On seeing me he 
ordered another knife and fork, which I declined; 
and it was well I did so, for alone, and without any 
assistance, he quite easily got rid of everything, 
leaving of the mutton nothing but the bone, of the 
fowl nothing but the skeleton, and of the salad no- 
thing but the bowl. Next they brought a cheese of 
considerable size and in it he made an angular 
breach of ninety degrees; the whole being washed 
down with a bottle of wine and a decanter of water, 
he then went to have his forty winks. 

One thing which delighted me was, that daring 
the whole of this performance, lasting nearly three 
quarters of an hour, the venerable pastor did not 
at all seem too much • enjgrossed in his work, The 
huge pieces which he threw into his capacious 
mouth prevented him neither from talking nor 
laughing, and he despatched all that was put before 
him with as little effort as if he had on^y eaten a 
couple of larks. 

In the same way General Bisson, who drank eight 
bottles of wine every day at breakfast, never seemed 
to be doing anything of lh^ sort. His glass was 
larger than the others, and he emptied it oflener; 
but you would have said that be did it without any 
effort, and, whilst thus imbibing his sixteen pints 
he could as freely join in pleasant chat or give his 
orders as if he had only drunk a single bottle. 



At the age of eighteen. General Sibuet had that 
happy appetite by which Nature announces her in- 
tention of completing a well developed man, when 
one evening he entered Genin's dining room8,where 
the worthies of the place usually met to eat chest- 
nuts over a bottle of white wine there called 
"cross grain." 

A superb turkey had just been taken off the spit, 
a fine bird, golden, done to a turn, and scenting the 
room enough to tempt a saint. 

The vilUge worthies, not being hungry, took very 
little notice of it; but the digestive powers ot young 
Sibuet were stirred within him, and with his mouth 
watering, he cried, "I have only just had dinner, 
yet I'll lay a bet to eat that big turkey all by 
myself." 

"Done!" replied Bouvier du Bouchet, a stout 
farmer, who happened to be in the room; if you'll 
eat it, I'll pay for you; but if you come to a halt, 
then you'll pay, and I'll eat the rest." 

Instantly setting to work, the yonng athlete de- 

tatched a wing skillfully and swallowed it in two 

^mouthfuls; then kept his teeth in play whilst taking 



Ik glass of wine as an interlude, by cranching the 
nec^ of the fowl. 

Next he tackled the thiish, and after eating it 
with the same self-possession, took a second glass 
of wine to clear the way for the remainder. Very 
soon the second wing went the same road, and on 
its disappearance, the performer, as keen as ever, 
was taking hold of the only remaining limb, when 
the unfortunate farmer shouted in a doleful tone, 
<'Ahl I see very well you'll win; but as I haye' to 
pay, leave me a small bit to myself." 

8ibuet was as good natured as he afterwards 
showed himse f courageous.and not only consented to 
his opponent's request, who thus had for his share 
the carcase of the fowl, but paid both for the turkey 
■and the necessary accompaniments.

Some Noted Epiourea. 



From All The Year Roand. 



Among noted epicures of this era — Louis XIV — 
were the Marquis de* Cussy, inventer of a cake 
which still bears his name; Camerani, a mediocre 
actor, but excellent stage manager of the Coroedie 
Italienne, who employed his leisure hours in the 
composition of a soup, the materials of which were 
80 costly as to be beyond the reach of the ordinary 
epicure; and Journiac de St. Meard, the same who 
during the Reign of Terror had miraculously escap- 
ed sharing the fate of his fellow-suspects in the 
prison of the Abbaye. According to contemporary 
accounts, it ir&s his custom to lake his place at the 
table early in the morning and never leave it before 
night; and it is recorded of him that, having invit- 
ed a friend to dinner, he pressed him to partake 
of a particular dish, which the other declined doing, 
pleading as an excuse that he feared it might not 
agree with him. <'BahI'* contemptuously exclaimed 
Journiac, "you don't mean to say that you are one 
of the idiots who trouble themselves about their di- 
gestion!'* 

Nor must a certain priest be forgotten, whose 
elasticity of conscience in culinary matters was pro- 
verbial. Being invited on a fast day to a repast 
befitting the occasion at the house of a noted lover 
of good cheer, he was on the point of helping him- 
self to a dish, the odor of which singularly tickled 
his palate, when the lay brother who accompanied 
him enjoined him in a whisper not to touch it, 
adding that he had seen it prepared in the kitchen 
and that the gravy was simply the essence of meat. 
"Meddling fool!" angrily muttered his superior, 
pushing away the dish wilh a sigh of mortification; 
"what business had be in the kitcken? Couldn't 
he have kept it to himself until after dinner?" 

We can remember many years ago conversing 
with an old gentleman who had been on intimate 



terms with Brillat-Savarin and Grimod de la Rey- 
niere> and questioning him about them. "Brillat- 
Savarin," he said, "was the pleasantest and cheer- 
iest of men, but he had one defeet; he was inordin- 
ately fond of pork, and I recollect a dinner given, 
by him at Villers-sur-Org^, on which occasion a 
delicately-prepared sucking-pig met with such gen- 
eral approbation that our host sent for (he cook, 
and after complimenting him on his skill, declared 
his intention of bestowing on him a suitable recom- 
pense, and having ascertained on' inquiry that M. 
Pierre's ambition was to marry a young girl whose 
face was her fortune, promised a handsome dowry 
to the bride, besides paying for the wedding din- 
ner; so that the sucking-pig eventually cost him 
over 6,000 francs." 

During the Consulate and the Empire the most 
fashionable "traiteur" was the Beauvi'liers, whose 
splendid dining rooms in the Rue Richelieu were 
frequented by the best society in Paris. Unlike 
the generality of his colleagues,^he was equally re- 
nowned for his polished and courteous manners 
and for the orthodox propriety of his costumes; he 
invariably received his customers himself, and took 
infinite pains that every thing set before them should 
be sufficiently tempting to induce them to repeat 
their visit. One day a gentleman, whom he recog- 
nized as a well known marquis, came in and order- 
ed a "supreme de volaille" (a specialty of the es- 
tablishment), which in due time was placed on the 
table. Beauvilliers, happening to pass by at the 
moment, glanced at the dish, and in spite of the re- 
monstrances of the marquis pounced upon it, and 
delivered it to a waiter, directing him to have an- 
other prepared immediately. Then, turning to his 
indignant visitor,and deliberately savouring a pinch 
of snufi^, "M. le Marquis," he said, "you will par- 
don the abruptness of my proceeding but the honor 
of my house is at stake. I regret that you should 
be exposed to a little temporary inconvenience, but 
I cannot allow my reputation to be compromised by 
a failure." 

When the illustrious academicians, Villemain and 
Victor Cousin, were young students, they generally 
dined together for the sake of economy, their 
modest repast consisting of a single dish of meat, 
with now and then a couple of apples, one for each; 
by way of dessert. On these gala occasions Ville- 
main, who had-a weakness for this supplementary 
luxury, never omitted to start a subject of conver- 
sation on which his companion loved to air his 
theories; and while the latter declaimed and philo- 
sophised to his heart's content, quiet y ate both the 
apples. 

To the foregoing list of gastronomic celebrities 
may be added the names of three men of mark of 
our own time, Balzac, Alexandre Dumas and 
Rossini. The first of these, although sufficiently 
abstemious in other respects, had an inordinate pre- 



diiection for pastry and fruit, devouriog, as Lecn 
Qox'an tells us, whole dishes of Montreuil peaQhes 
%nd juicy pears with Gaigantuan facility. Dumas 
Musiderel bis culinary manual a masterpiece far 
saperior to tne r<Icu«f^uetaires or Monte Cristo, 
while the composer of II Barbiere was ne?er so 
happy as when superintending the preparation of a 
dish invented by himself* **I was born to be a 
cook," he exclaimed one evening, while presiding 
at the supper-table of his rilla at Passy; "and have 
altogether missed my vocation I" 

**But, maestro," objected one of his guests, "in 
that case we should have had no Guillaume 
Tell." 

"Bah!" contemptuously retorted Rossini, "any- 
one could have done that.' Donizetti and Bellini 
can write operas, but if either of them were to try 
his hand at a *timbale de macaroni aux truffes, " 
helping himself largely as he spoke to the delicacy 
in question, "do you imagine for a moment that it 
would taste like this."

Lovers of Truffles. 



Epicurean Olergy 



It is a remarkable fact that* the epicures of the 
world should be so largely indebted to the French 
clergy for the luxuries they enjoy. It has been sug- 
gested that during the long season of Lent these 
holy men have been in the habit of relieving their 
privations by employing their ingenuity in the in- 
vention of pleasant foods and drinks in readiness 
for the return of the days of feasting. Whether there 
is any foundation for this or not is not positively 
known, but the fact remains that the clergy, from 
whatever cause, are capital inventors of all comes- 
tibles. One of the largest oyster parks in the 
country was started by the Abbe Bonnetard, the 
cure of La Teste, whose system of artificial cultiva- 
tion was so successful that last year, of 161,000,000 
oysters distributed through France, 97,000,000 
-t'jere produced by the abbe. Canon Agen was the 
discoverer of the terrines of Ihe Nerac. The rilettes 
jf Tours are the work of a monk of Marmoutiers. 
The renowned liqeurs Chartreuse, Trappestine, 
Benedictine,and others betray their monastic origin 
in their names, and the strangest part of their pro- 
duction is that they should be the work of the most 
severe and ascetic of religtous bodies. The Elixir 
of Garus is the invention of the Abbe Garus. The 
Beziers sausages were first prepared under the 
direction of the Prior Lamouroux. The popular 
Bergougnous sauce was first mingled by the Abbe 
Bergougnous. The delicate Floguard cakes are the 
invention of the Abbe Floguard. Even the immor- 
tal glory of the discovery of champagne is attributed 
to a monk. To these may be added the innumer- 
able delicacies in bon-bons, confectionery, and the 
like, which owe their origin entirely to the nuns 



London Telegraph. 

Herr Julius Olden, a contemporary eulogist of 
the truffle, boldly asserts that ever since the dis* 
CO very of this toothsome tuber it has been beloved 
of poets and musicians above a' I other comestibles^ 
Among its most renowned votaries he assigns front- 
rank places to Georges Sand — who bestowed upoa 
it the fanciful title of "Fair Potato," and immor. 
talized its merits in a metrical legend — Lord Byron 
and Rossini. Byron's extravagant fondness for 
macaroni has been recorded in more than one sketch 
of his tastes and habits; but his biographers, ac- 
cording to Herr Olden, have omitted to mention the 
fact that he was want to bestrew his macaroni so 
thickly with slices of truffle that the result — his 
favorite dish — might have been more correctly de- 
scribed as "truffles au macaroni" than as "maca- 
roni aux truffles." The Swan Pesaro was no less 
enthusiastic a truffle-worshiper than the author of 
'<Don Juan." It was Rossini whose fertile brain,, 
stimulated to superhuman activity by dread of an 
impending gastronomical calamity, invented truffle 
salad. He was dining one day with several cele" 
brated epicures, at the table of Baron James Roths- 
child. The moment had arrived for serving the 
roti, when it was discovered to the horror of all 
present, that the Baron's chef had forgotten ta 
provide any salad I Rossini was the only person 
who preserved his presence of mind. He called for 
truffles and the castors, cut up the former into deli- 
cate slices, mixed a sublime dressing with the con- 
tents of the latter, and in a few minutes produced: 
a salad of so seductively delicate a flavor that his 
admiring fellow-gourmets unanimously christened 
it "the poetry of truffles set to music by Maestro 
Rossini." 



The following anecdote of the Count Vittalio 
Borromeo and his famous chef is related: "The 
Count was a great epicure, and would sooner part 
with his best friend than with his cook. This cul- 
nary artist knew how to please his master with a 
variety of dishes, known only to himself, in fact his 
own production, among which was one, that for the 
delicacy of its meat, the aroma of its condiment,and 
the general care taken in its preparation, made it 
the favorite above all the rest, and for many years 
it held the monopoly of the Count's table. The 
cook died, and many filled his place, but to no sat^ 
isfaction. Money was freely lavished but to no 
purpose. The Count was languishing for want of 
an appetite, until one day, after a careful investi- 
^gation an old scullion, for many years an inmate of 



the palace, conducted the new cook to a remote 
sub-cellar of the palace,iu a comer of which a large 
cage was built, and continually supplied with enor- 
mous rats, fed on meal and milk, fattened and 
purified, until ready to kill. These, and the ingenu- 
ity of the cook, kept for a long time the old Count 
in ecstasy over his table.''

ceived a visit a few days ago from his landlord. It

The proprietor listened atttentively and promising

"Six o'clock already,** said the landlord. 

'•Exactly," replied VI. de Rumilly; <*but that 
doesn't matter, fori hope you will do me the honor 
of dining with me." 

"fou are very kind," replied the landlord, 
"but—" 

"I insist; I shall not let you leave at this hour; 
your plate is already laid." 

'*It is impossible." 

*<I shall be angry." 

"It is impossible, notwithstanding the desire 1 
have to remain. My affairs call me elsewhere at 
precisely this hour." 

"You do not wish to share my dinner?" said the 
senator, slightly vexed. 

"You will understand why. They dine at M. de 
Rothschild's at 7 o'clock." 

"Ah, you are his guest 1 

"No, I am his cook."

Boznan Oooks and Gourmands. 



Quarterly Review. 

In Juvenal's time the salary of a good cook was 
ten times higher than that of a tutor,aman of learn- 
ing and ability, who, according to Lucian, was 
deemed well paid with 200 sesterces a year. The 
salary of Dionysia, a danseuse, was 200,000. The 
houses and establishments of the two players in 
pantomime, Bathyllus and Pylades, rivaled those 
of the richest patricians. There were three Romans 
named Apicius, each celebrated for devotion to gas- 
tronomy. The second, who flourished under Tiber- 
ius, was the most famous, and enjoys the credit of 
having shown both discrimination and industry in 
the gratification of his appetite; so much so that his 
name has passed into a synonym for an aooom- 
plished epicure. After spending aboat £800,000 
OB his palate be balanced his books, and finding 



that he had not much more than £80,00t left, hang* 
ed himself to avoid living; .upon such a pittance 
Lcmpriere's version is that he made a mistake im 
casting up his books, and hanged himse f under a 
false impjession of insolvency. A noted betting 
man named Smith made a similar mistake in casting 
up his boo'« for the Derby, and flung himself into^ 
the sea- He was fished out, discovered the mistaker 
and ever since went by the name of Neptune Smiths 
Apicius unluckily had no kind friend to cut hici» 
down. The outrageous absurdities of Elagabalus- 
equaled or surpassed those of Caligula and Nero;- 
He fed the officers of his palace with the brains oi 
pheasants and thrushes, the eggs of partridges, and 
the heads of parrots. Among the dishes served at 
his own table were peas mashed with grains of gold , 
beans fricasseed with morsels of amber, and rice 
mixed with pearls. His meals were frequently com- 
posed of twenty-two services. Turning roofs threw 
flowers with such profusion on the guests that they 
were nearly smothered. At the sea-side he never 
ate fish, but when far inland he caused the roe of 
the rarest to be distributed among his suite. He 
was the first Roman who ever wore a complete dresa 
of silk. His shoes glittered with rubies and emer- 
alds, and his chariots were of gold, inlaid with 
precious stones. With a view to becoming suicide- 
he had cords of purple silk, poisons inclosed ir 
emeralds, and richly set daggers; but either h''> 
courage failed when the moment arrived for choos- 
ing between these elegant instruments of death, or 
no time was left him for the choice. He was killed 
in an insurrection of the soldiery in the eighteenth 
year of his age, after a reign of nearly four years, 
during which the Roman people had endured the 
insane and degrading tyranny of a boy.

Ben. Butler is one of the biggest eaters that visik 
the Fifth Avenue Hotel. He devours an enormous 
quantity of meat, vegetables, milk, coffee, salads 
and sweets. A chicken disappears before him as 
though he was a Methodist preacher and it was a^ 
partridge. He doesn't affect wines. At his hom» 
he has several Tarieties upon his table, but he 
drinks only about a tumblerful of sherry. If he 
wants a drink he takes a glass of Jam^iica rum, or 
the statesman's drink, brandy. He eats four meals 
a day, and is never troubled with indigestion. He 
smokes cigars that are strong enough to knock a 
marine over. Tet he prides himself upon his tem- 
perate life» to which he traces his prosperity.
